1 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Crooks v. Mabus


No. 15-5212


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


845 F.3d 412; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 23180


October 14, 2016, Submitted 
December 23, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Crooks v. Mabus, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 2122 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 6, 2017)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Crooks v. Mabus, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 2120 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 6, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cv-01614).
Crooks v. Mabus, 104 F. Supp. 3d 86, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 65590 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Commander, Navy Education and Training Command Instruction 1533.9K, art. 404(e)(5), which provided for revocation of a Navy Junior Reserve Officers' Training Corps instructor's certification if continued certification was not in the best interests of the program, was not void for vagueness, as the standard was given context by the factors the Navy considered; [2]-The Navy did not violate a former instructor's procedural due process rights by decertifying him. His position did not create a protected property interest, and even there was any imposition of stigma constituting infringement on a protected liberty interest, the instructor was afforded all of the process that he was due; [3]-The decertification decision was neither arbitrary and capricious nor unsupported by substantial evidence in light of evidence regarding the instructor's performance.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

2 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Autor v. Pritzker


No. 16-5037


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


843 F.3d 994; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 22345


October 11, 2016, Argued 
December 16, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-01593).
Autor v. Blank, 128 F. Supp. 3d 331, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 121725 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Plaintiffs did not meet the appellate court's three-party test under the Buckhannon decision for being a prevailing party; [2]-They overread the Autor I decision as the appellate court acknowledged that on remand dismissal might still be appropriate depending on the district court's disposition of the government's remaining defense; [3]-While the Autor I decision narrowed the grounds on which the government could defend the lobbyist ban, it did not foreclose the possibility that the government could prevail on the merits; [4]-No judicial determination had been made on the merits of their constitutional challenges before the government revised the lobbyist ban; [5]-While they achieved a desired result, their success in the Autor I decision lacked the necessary judicial imprimatur on the merits of their challenge to the lobbyist ban to secure the status of prevailing party.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

3 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Redrick


No. 14-3053


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


841 F.3d 478; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 20106


September 7, 2016, Argued 
November 8, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by United States v. Redrick, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 1558 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 27, 2017)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Redrick, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 1557 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 27, 2017)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Redrick v. United States, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 3477 (U.S., May 30, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cr-00155-1).
United States v. Redrick, 48 F. Supp. 3d 91, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 91059 (D.D.C., July 3, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where defendant pled guilty to being a felon in unlawful possession of a firearm, the trial court properly enhanced his sentence under the Armed Career Criminal Act based on his three prior convictions of violent felonies; it was immaterial that an intervening opinion, Johnson v. United States, held that the definition of a "violent felony" in the Act's residual clause was unconstitutionally vague, because defendant's conviction in Maryland of robbery with deadly weapon was a violent felony under the Act's force clause, which remained valid.


OUTCOME: The sentence was affirmed.

5 of 475 DOCUMENTS


PHH Corp. v. Consumer Fin. Prot. Bureau


No. 15-1177


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


839 F.3d 1; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 18332; Fed. Sec. L. Rep. (CCH) P99,434


April 12, 2016, Argued 
October 11, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Rehearing, en banc, granted by PHH Corp. v. Consumer Fin. Prot. Bureau, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 2733 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 16, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petition for Review of an Order of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau. (CFPB File 2014-CFPB-0002).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau (CFPB) was unconstitutionally structured under U.S. Const. art. II, § 1 because it was an independent agency headed by a single director; to remedy the constitutional flaw, the court severed the unconstitutional for-cause provision, so that the president would have the power to remove the director at will and to supervise and direct the director; [2]-The Real Estate Settlement Procedures Act § 8(a), 12 U.S.C.S. § 2607(a) allows captive reinsurance arrangements if the amount paid by the mortgage insurer for the reinsurance does not exceed the reasonable market value; [3]-A CFPB order that a lender pay $109 million for past captive reinsurance arrangements violated due process by retroactively applying a new interpretation of § 8; [4]-A three-year statute of limitations applies to all CFPB actions to enforce § 8.


OUTCOME: Petition granted, order vacated, and remanded for further proceedings.

 

6 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Heartland Plymouth Court MI, LLC v. NLRB


No. 15-1034


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


838 F.3d 16; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 17688; 207 L.R.R.M. 3321; 167 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P10,953



September 30, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petitioner's Motion for Attorney Fees.
Heartland Plymouth Court MI, LLC v. NLRB, 650 Fed. Appx. 11, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 8164 (D.C. Cir., May 3, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-After petitioner employer obtained review of respondent NLRB's (Board) order and the order's enforcement was denied, the Board's nonacquiescence to the court's decision was improper because 29 U.S.C.S. § 160(f) venue uncertainty did not excuse its less-than-candid representation of its disagreement with adverse circuit law, its failure to preserve its arguments for U.S. Supreme Court review, or its failure to seek certiorari review to achieve a national resolution; [2]-Its nonacquiescence was bad faith, authorizing an award of attorney fees under 28 U.S.C.S. § 2412(b), because it showed persistent nonacquiescence without candor or the pursuit of judicial finality and did not invoke its right to transfer the case to a more favorable court, and its obstinacy forced the employer to waste time and resources to obtain a result it knew the court's precedent would provide.


OUTCOME: Motion granted.

 

7 of 475 DOCUMENTS


League of Women Voters of the United States v. Newby


No. 16-5196


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


838 F.3d 1; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 17463


September 8, 2016, Argued 
September 26, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:16-cv-00236).
League of Women Voters of the United States v. Newby, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 16835 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 9, 2016)
League of Women Voters of the United States v. Newby, 195 F. Supp. 3d 80, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 84727 (D.D.C., June 29, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-As a result of the decisions of the Director of the Election Assistance Commission (Commission), it was more difficult for the organizations to accomplish their primary mission of registering voters, which provided irreparable harm because after the registration deadlines had passed, there could be no redress; [2]-The organizations had a substantial likelihood of success on the merits because the Commission had the authority to determine whether information was necessary within the meaning of 52 U.S.C.S. § 20508; [3]-A preliminary injunction would serve the public interest because, absent an injunction, there was a substantial risk that citizens would be disenfranchised in the present federal election cycle; [4]-The diminished ability of the organizations to use the Federal Form in voter registration drives ran contrary to what Congress declared to be the public interest.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

8 of 475 DOCUMENTS


DirecTV, Inc. v. NLRB


No. 11-1273 Consolidated with 11-1274, 11-1294


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


837 F.3d 25; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 16940; 207 L.R.R.M. 3251


September 8, 2015, Argued 
September 16, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by DirecTV, Inc. v. NLRB, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 2462 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 10, 2017)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by DirecTV, Inc. v. NLRB, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 2463 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 10, 2017)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Mastec Advanced Techs. v. NLRB, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 5318 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petitions for Review and Cross-Application for Enforcement of an Order of the National Labor Relations Board.
MasTec Advanced Techs., 357 N.L.R.B. 103, 2011 NLRB LEXIS 367 (July 21, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-An order of the National Labor Relations Board under 29 U.S.C.S. § 160(e) and (f), which held that it was an unfair labor practice under 29 U.S.C.S. § 158(a)(1) to terminate the employees for airing their pay policy grievances publicly, such that they were to be reinstated, was supported by substantial evidence that the employees had engaged in protected activities under 29 U.S.C.S. § 157; [2]-The Board's holding that the employees' dissatisfaction with the new pay policy, which led to their statements in a television interview, constituted protected concerted activity, and that their statements were not so disloyal or so maliciously untrue that they fell outside of the protection of the National Labor Relations Act, was based on substantial evidence, such that it was upheld on review.


OUTCOME: Petitions for review denied; cross-application for enforcement granted.

 

9 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Rothe Dev., Inc. v. United States DOD


No. 15-5176


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


836 F.3d 57; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 16556


March 10, 2016, Argued 
September 9, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Rothe Dev. v. United States DOD, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 728 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 13, 2017)
Rehearing denied by, En banc Rothe Dev., Inc. v. United States DOD, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 730 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 13, 2017)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Rothe Dev., Inc. v. DOD, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 6278 (U.S., Oct. 16, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-00744).
Rothe Dev., Inc. v. DOD, 107 F. Supp. 3d 183, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 72925 (D.D.C., June 5, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The preference given to businesses owned by socially and economically disadvantaged individuals under Small Business Act § 8(a) (15 U.S.C.S. § 637(a)) was facially race-neutral and not subject to strict scrutiny review in an equal protection challenge because eligibility was based on individual experiences of bias and could include business owners of any race or ethnicity, nothing in 15 U.S.C.S. § 644(g)(1)(A)(iv) required defining owners by race, and the policy findings in 15 U.S.C.S. § 631(f) concerning race-based disadvantage did not impose any race-based classification or presumption; [2]-Even if the statute could be read to permit the use of a racial presumption, the canon of constitutional avoidance directed that it not be so construed; [3]-In aiming to remedy the effects of bias and prejudice, § 8(a) had a rational basis; [4]-A nondelegation challenge lacked merit.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

10 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re Al-Nashiri


No. 15-1023 Consolidated with 15-5020


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


835 F.3d 110; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 15974


February 17, 2016, Argued 
August 30, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by In re Abd Al-Rahim Hussein Muhammed Al-Nashiri, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 18909 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 19, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by al-Nashiri v. Trump, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 6288 (U.S., Oct. 16, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petition for Writ of Mandamus and Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-01207).
Abd Al-Rahim Hussain Mohammed Al-Nashiri v. Obama, 76 F. Supp. 3d 218, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 177736 (D.D.C., Dec. 29, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Appellant/petitioner detainee was not entitled to preliminarily enjoin a military commission's (commission) proceedings because abstention was proper, as the Military Commission Act's protections of his rights in 10 U.S.C.S. § 948a et seq. exceeded those found adequate in the context of courts-martial, an important countervailing interest justified abstention in the area of national security, and no exception to abstention applied; [2]-He was not entitled to mandamus relief barring a commission's exercise of jurisdiction because he showed no clear and indisputable right by claiming no hostilities supporting jurisdiction existed at the time of his alleged acts, given questions as to whether this was based on the totality of the circumstances or the understanding of the political branches and on a retrospective analysis, or only on what decisionmakers believed at the time.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; petition denied.

 

11 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Jones v. Kirchner


No. 14-5257 Consolidated with 15-5088


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


835 F.3d 74; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 15759


March 21, 2016, Argued 
August 26, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jones v. Kirchner, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 1934 (U.S., Mar. 20, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-01334).
Jones v. Kirchner, 66 F. Supp. 3d 237, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 125226 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In plaintiff's § 1983 action against police officers, he made a prima facie showing that the offers violated the Fourth Amendment; [2]-Plaintiff's allegation of unlawful seizure was also plausible. Fed. R. Civ. P. 8 did not require plaintiff, before discovery, to identify each seized item and document and to show that each fell outside the scope of the warrant; [3]-Because the District of Columbia was the forum "state" in the case, pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, District of Columbia law provided the applicable statute of limitation. From the time the officers entered plaintiff's home, he was not free to leave, so was imprisoned for purposes of tolling under D.C. Code § 12-302(a)(3); [4]-Officers were entitled to qualified immunity because it was not clearly established in Maryland in 2005 that the Fourth Amendment prohibited nighttime execution of a daytime-only warrant.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part. Reversed in part and remanded.

 

12 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Aref v. Lynch


No. 15-5154


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


833 F.3d 242; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 15230


March 15, 2016, Argued 
August 19, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00539).
Aref v. Holder, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 79636 (D.D.C., Mar. 16, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The indefinite duration of confinement in and atypicality of federal prison communication management units (CMUs), in which family visits and communications with the outside world were curtailed, gave rise to a Fifth Amendment liberty interest requiring due process; prisoners who had spent several years housed in CMU units were entitled to a determination of whether they were afforded sufficient process; [2]-Prisoners can recover compensatory damages under the Prison Litigation Reform Act (PLRA) for injuries that are neither mental nor emotional; [3]-Prisoners who allege actual harms that are neither mental nor emotional are entitled to punitive damages if they can show the defendant's conduct was motivated by evil motive or intent or involved reckless or callous indifference; [4]-The chief of the counter-terrorism unit was entitled to qualified immunity on the PLRA claims.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment affirmed in part; reversed and remanded in part.

 

13 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Sheffield


No. 12-3013


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


832 F.3d 296; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 14826; 101 Fed. R. Evid. Serv. (Callaghan) 182


May 20, 2016, Argued 
August 12, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cr-00213-1).
United States v. Sheffield, 76 F. Supp. 3d 148, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 176640 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-There was no error in the district court's denial of defendant's suppression motion with respect to phencyclidine that was found during a search of a car that defendant was a passenger in, as the police had probable cause under U.S. Const. amend. IV to lawfully stop the vehicle due to observing traffic violations, and there was a smell of marijuana and numerous air fresheners that supported the search of the car; [2]-Moreover, suppression of statements made by defendant following his arrest, but before he received Miranda warnings, was not warranted because they were not made in response to an interrogation or questioning by the officers; [3]-However, application of the career-offender enhancement under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 4B1.1(a) was concededly error, as defendant's prior attempted robbery under D.C. Code § 22-2802 was not a crime of violence.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed as to conviction; reversed as to career-offender enhancement. Sentence vacated; matter remanded for resentencing.

 

14 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. District of Columbia


No. 14-7210


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


832 F.3d 339; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 14825; 41 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1006


November 18, 2015, Argued 
August 12, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:97-cv-01015).
Thompson v. Dist. of Columbia, 530 F.3d 914, 382 U.S. App. D.C. 1, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 13547 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court erred, on remand, in granting summary judgment to an employer in the public employee's action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, alleging that his termination from employment due to having been transferred to a position that was immediately eliminated in a reduction in force violated his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. V, as the employee established that his due process rights were violated and that such violation caused his alleged damages; [2]-The employee was deprived of a protected interest in his former position when he was transferred to a position that was already marked for elimination, and he was deprived of the process he was due because he received no notice of the reassignment and was constructively terminated at the time of the transfer.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment reversed; matter remanded with instructions to enter partial summary judgment for employee and for further proceedings.

 

15 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Jawad v. Gates


No. 15-5250


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


832 F.3d 364; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 14823


April 5, 2016, Argued 
August 12, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jawad v. Gates, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 2967 (U.S., May 15, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-00811).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the Military Commissions Act of 2006, 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e)(2), barred the district court from exercising jurisdiction over a former detainee's damages claims arising from his detention as an enemy combatant. HOLDINGS: [1]-A Combatant Status Review Tribunal had found the detainee to be an enemy combatant, which satisfied the requirement under § 2241(e)(2) that he be determined to have been properly detained as an enemy combatant; [2]-The government's notice in the detainee's habeas action that it would no longer treat him as detainable did not amount to a concession that he had never been properly detained; [3]-The detainee did not explain how any domestic law or treaty prohibited detention of juvenile enemy combatants; [4]-The jurisdictional bar was not limited to suits by unlawful enemy combatants.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Raymond J. Lucia Cos. v. SEC


No. 15-1345


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


832 F.3d 277; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 14559; Fed. Sec. L. Rep. (CCH) P99,255


May 13, 2016, Argued 
August 9, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Rehearing, en banc, granted by Raymond J. Lucia Cos. v. SEC, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 2732 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 16, 2017)
Decision reached on appeal by, Petition denied by, En banc Raymond J. Lucia Companies, Inc. v. SEC, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 11298 (D.C. Cir., June 26, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petition for Review of an Order of the Securities & Exchange Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Securities and Exchange Commission (Commission) did not abuse its discretion by imposing the sanction of a lifetime industry bar since the Commission considered the proposed alternative sanctions and determined, in its judgment, that they would not have been sufficient to protect investors and justification existed based on the advisor's repeated and reckless engagement in egregious conduct without regard to his fiduciary duty to his clients; [2]-The court also held that Commission Administrative Law Judges (ALJs) were not officers within the meaning of the Appointments Clause, U.S. Const. art. II, § 2, cl. 2.


OUTCOME: Petition denied.

 

17 of 475 DOCUMENTS


True the Vote, Inc. v. IRS


No. 14-5316, No. 15-5013


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


831 F.3d 551; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 14375; 2016-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50,373; 118 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 5367


April 14, 2016, Argued 
August 5, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by True the Vote, Inc. v. Lerner, 137 S. Ct. 1068, 197 L. Ed. 2d 177, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 1099 (U.S., Feb. 21, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cv-00734). Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cv-00777).
Linchpins of Liberty v. United States, 71 F. Supp. 3d 236, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 151124 (D.D.C., Oct. 23, 2014)
True the Vote, Inc. v. IRS, 71 F. Supp. 3d 219, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 151072 (D.D.C., Oct. 23, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a case in which the IRS afforded unequal treatment in the processing of applications for tax exempt status under 26 U.S.C.S. 501(c)(3) and (4) by applicants whose names might suggest certain political orientations, the circuit court concluded that the district court properly dismissed plaintiffs' Bivens claims and their statutory claims under 26 U.S.C.S. § 6103; [2]-However, the district court erred in concluding that the litigation over the government's conduct had been mooted by the government's putative voluntary cessation of the conduct; [3]-The government's heavy burden of showing mootness under the voluntary cessation doctrine required that the government establish cessation, not near cessation.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for further proceedings.

 

18 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Weinstein v. Islamic Republic of Iran


No. 14-7193, Consolidated with 14-7194, 14-7195, 14-7198, 14-7202, 14-7203, 14-7204


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


831 F.3d 470; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 13981


January 21, 2016, Argued 
August 2, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Weinstein v. Islamic Republic of Iran, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 17160 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 19, 2016)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Weinstein v. Islamic Republic of Iran, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 17159 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 19, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:00-cv-02601), (No. 1:02-cv-01811), (No. 1:08-cv-00520), (No. 1:01-cv-01655), (No. 1:08-cv-00502), (No. 1:00-cv-02602), (No. 1:14-mc-00648).
Stern v. Islamic Republic of Iran, 73 F. Supp. 3d 46, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 159360 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Victims of government-sponsored terrorist attacks holding unsatisfied judgments against the governments were precluded from attaching Internet data by requiring the Internet Corporation for Assigned Names and Numbers (ICANN) to delegate management of country-code top level domain names, since such attachment would impair the substantial interests of ICANN in protecting the stability and interoperability of the domain name system; [2]-The proposed attachment would also undermine the Internet's stability and interoperability by allowing the potentially disastrous formation by operators of top level domains of a competitor to ICANN which was otherwise globally accepted.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

19 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Pursuing America's Greatness v. FEC


No. 15-5264


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


831 F.3d 500; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 13979


February 23, 2016, Argued 
August 2, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:15-cv-01217).
Pursuing America's Greatness v. FEC, 132 F. Supp. 3d 23, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 128250 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A claim of a political committee, which was not authorized to receive or spend money on behalf of a candidate, that a regulation prohibiting the use of the candidate's name in the committee's website and social media was unconstitutional was not moot based on the candidate's withdrawal from a campaign, since the committee intended to support other candidates in other races; [2]-There was a substantial likelihood that the regulation violated the committee's freedom of speech since the prohibition on the use of the candidate's name was a restriction on the committee's political speech based on the message conveyed, and the regulation was content based regardless of its stated purpose of avoiding voter confusion; [3]-The restriction was not narrowly tailored since the avoidance of voter confusion could be achieved by requiring a distinct and express disclaimer.


OUTCOME: Order reversed.

21 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Patchak v. Jewell


No. 15-5200


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


828 F.3d 995; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 12984


May 13, 2016, Argued 
July 15, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, in part, Motion granted by Patchak v. Zinke, 137 S. Ct. 2091, 197 L. Ed. 2d 893, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 2894 (U.S., May 1, 2017)
Motion granted by Patchak v. Zinke, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 6107 (U.S., Oct. 10, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-01331).
Patchak v. Jewell, 109 F. Supp. 3d 152, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 78553 (D.D.C., June 16, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Appellant landowner's suit contesting appellee Department of the Interior's (Department) taking of land in trust for appellee tribe failed because the Gun Lake Trust Land Reaffirmation Act (Act), Pub. L. No. 113-179, 128 Stat. 1913 (2014), permissibly removed federal jurisdiction, as the Act constitutionally exercised Congress's power to legislate as to Indian tribes; [2]-The Act did not violate the landowner's right to petition because Congress could withhold federal jurisdiction; [3]-The Act did not violate his due process rights because the legislation provided all process that was due; [4]-The Act was not a U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 3 bill of attainder because its means were rationally designed to meet its legitimate nonpunitive goals; [5]-It was not an abuse of discretion to deny a motion to strike a record supplementation because the court had no jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

22 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Loumiet v. United States


No. 15-5208


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


828 F.3d 935; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 12760


March 24, 2016, Argued 
July 12, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-01130).
Loumiet v. United States, 968 F. Supp. 2d 142, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 130286 (D.D.C., Sept. 12, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court concluded, in line with the majority of its sister circuits to have considered the question, that the discretionary-function exception, 28 U.S.C.S. § 2680(a), does not categorically bar Federal Tort Claims Act (FTCA) tort claims where the challenged exercise of discretion allegedly exceeded the government's constitutional authority to act. The district court erred as a matter of law in barring plaintiff attorney's FTCA claims on the ground that, as a general matter, "even constitutionally defective" exercises of discretion fall within the Act's discretionary-function exception; [2]-Nor were the attorney's Bivens claims time-barred, because the continuing-violations doctrine applied to extend the applicable statute of limitations where, as here, a plaintiff alleged continuing conduct causing cumulative harm.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal order of the district court and remanded for further proceedings.

 

23 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Williams


No. 13-3019 Consolidated with 13-3035, 14-3012


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


827 F.3d 1134; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 12551; 100 Fed. R. Evid. Serv. (Callaghan) 1041


March 11, 2016, Argued 
July 8, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Williams, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 23540 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 17, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Edwards v. United States, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 323 (U.S., Jan. 9, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cr-00129-11), (No. 1:11-cr-00129-2), (No. 1:11-cr-00129-1).
United States v. Edwards, 994 F. Supp. 2d 11, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 24254 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a trial for participation in a cocaine distribution scheme, it was reversible error under Fed. R. Evid. 701 to admit lay opinion testimony from a special agent interpreting recorded conversations and other interactions between the alleged coconspirators because the testimony failed to establish the bases for the opinion and blurred the distinction between lay and expert opinion; [2]-It was not necessary to suppress evidence gathered as result of wiretap surveillance: no Franks hearing was required because omissions in the wiretap applications (regarding use of a pen register and a confidential informant) were not material; despite the omissions, the applications were supported by necessity because the government demonstrated that surveillance and undercover operations were unlikely to provide the source of defendant's cocaine or the hierarchy of his organization.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part; reversed and remanded in part.

 

24 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Akiachak Native Cmty. v. United States DOI


No. 13-5360


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


827 F.3d 100; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 12121; 46 ELR 20118


March 4, 2016, Argued 
July 1, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:06-cv-00969).
Akiachak Native Cmty. v. Jewell, 995 F. Supp. 2d 7, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 86931 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where several Indian Tribes sued the U.S. Department of Interior challenging a regulation implementing certain settled land claims staked by descendants of Alaskan aboriginal Tribes, and where after a district court held that the Department's interpretation was contrary to law, the Department, following notice and comment, revised its regulations and dismissed its appeal, the appeal of the State of Alaska, which had intervened in the district court as a party defendant but brought no independent claim for relief, was dismissed for lack of jurisdiction because the controversy between the Tribes and the Department was moot; [2]-The underlying controversy was moot under U.S. Const. art. III, § 2 because the Department's new regulation resolved the underlying claim by removing the Alaska exception from its land-into-trust regulations and there was no longer a live controversy.


OUTCOME: Decision vacated; appeal dismissed.

 

25 of 475 DOCUMENTS


W. Va. ex rel. Morrisey v. United States Dep't of HHS


No. 15-5309


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


827 F.3d 81; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 12123


April 15, 2016, Argued 
July 1, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by W. Va. ex rel. Morrisey v. HHS, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 2498 (U.S., Apr. 17, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-01287).
State of West Virginia v. United States HHS, 145 F. Supp. 3d 94, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 147380 (D.D.C., Oct. 30, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Although the State of West Virginia challenged the federal government's decision to decline enforcement, it conceded at oral argument that its claim of injury-in-fact was identical to that which would exist if Congress had initially provided that only States had authority to enforce the federal mandate; [2]-The grant of that discretion to the States did not create an injury-in-fact; [3]-West Virginia unsuccessfully sought to extend the Printz decision and the New York decision to the proposition that when the federal government abandons enforcement of a federal statute, leaving States with the responsibility, or, for that matter, Congress delegates discretion to implement a federal statute directly to a state, that was unconstitutional; [4]-Its injury was nothing more than the political discomfort in having the responsibility to determine whether to enforce or not.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

26 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Swaters v. United States DOT


No. 14-1277


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


826 F.3d 507; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 11556


January 22, 2016, Argued 
June 24, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petition for Review of an Order of the United States Department of Transportation.
Swaters v. Osmus, 568 F.3d 1315, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 11112 (11th Cir., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Petitioner pilot was incorrect in claiming the DOT failed to provide an adequate explanation for its decision. As the Department correctly pointed out, DOT officials repeatedly explained the agency's long-standing prohibition against the release of (urine) samples for DNA testing, and cited and extensively quoted the relevant regulations to document their position; [2]-The meaning of 49 C.F.R. § 40.13(c) is clear on its face: a laboratory is prohibited from making a DOT urine specimen available for a DNA test or other types of specimen identity testing; [3]-The Rule is not arbitrary or capricious; [4]-The Rule does not violate the Omnibus Act; [5]-Petitioner's constitutional arguments also lacked merit. In the absence of any limiting terms, the plain--and certainly not vague--meaning of the regulation prohibits release of the urine sample to anybody, 49 C.F.R. § 40.13(c).


OUTCOME: The petition for review was denied.

United States Telecom Ass'n v. FCC


No. 15-1063, Consolidated with 15-1078, 15-1086, 15-1090, 15-1091, 15-1092, 15-1095, 15-1099, 15-1117, 15-1128, 15-1151, 15-1164


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


825 F.3d 674; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 10716; 64 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 1663


December 4, 2015, Argued 
June 14, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by United States Telecom Ass'n v. FCC, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 7712 (D.C. Cir., May 1, 2017)
Petition for certiorari filed at, 09/28/2017


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Petitions for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
In re FCC Releases Open Internet R&O, Declaratory Ruling, & Order, 30 F.C.C.R. 5601, 2015 FCC LEXIS 731 (F.C.C., Mar. 12, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Federal Communications Commission did not violate § 553 of the Administrative Procedure Act, 5 U.S.C.S. § 553, in its reclassification of broadband service as a telecommunications service; [2]-The court lacked jurisdiction to entertain a broadband provider's argument that the Commission violated the Regulatory Flexibility Act, 5 U.S.C.S. § 604(a), by failing to conduct an adequate Final Regulatory Flexibility Analysis regarding the effects of reclassification on small businesses because the provider had not filed a petition for reconsideration as required by 47 U.S.C.S. § 405(a); [3]-The Commission had statutory authority under § 706 of the Telecommunications Act of 1996, 47 U.S.C.S. § 1302, to reclassify broadband as a telecommunications service, and it adequately explained why it reclassified broadband from an information service to a telecommunications service.


OUTCOME: Petitions for review denied.

Friends of Animals v. Jewell


No. 15-5070


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


824 F.3d 1033; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 10094; 82 ERC (BNA) 1689


February 22, 2016, Argued 
June 3, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Friends of Animals v. Jewell, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 6664 (U.S., Oct. 31, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-00357).
Friends of Animals v. Jewell, 82 F. Supp. 3d 265, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 25803 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The nonprofit organization's statutory and constitutional claims directly implicated 16 U.S.C.S. § 1539's disclosure requirement, which provided the nonprofit with a right to information, and having been denied such information, the nonprofit suffered a concrete and particularized injury in fact and had standing to pursue its claims; [2]-Appropriations Act, div. G, tit. I, § 127 obviously changed the reach of 16 U.S.C.S. §§ 1538 and 1539 of the Endangered Species Act; [3]-Following the passage of Section 127 and issuance of the Reinstatement Rule, the requirements in Section 10 that otherwise had to be met to obtain an exemption from Section 9's take prohibitions no longer applied to U.S. Captive-Bred herds of the three antelope species, even though they applied to other endangered species.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

29 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Duberry v. District of Columbia


No. 15-7062


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


824 F.3d 1046; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 10096


March 14, 2016, Argued 
June 3, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-01258).
Duberry v. District of Columbia, 106 F. Supp. 3d 245, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 68881 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Retired correctional officers stated a claim by alleging that the District of Columbia deprived them of their federal right under the Law Enforcement Officers Safety Act (LEOSA), 18 U.S.C.S. § 926C, to carry a concealed weapon by refusing to certify that, as correctional officers, they had a statutory power of arrest; [2]-The LEOSA creates the type of right remediable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983; [3]-"Statutory powers of arrest" under the LEOSA means some statutory power of arrest such as power to arrest parole violators, and not only police power to arrest upon probable cause; [4]-The LEOSA does not mandate that in order to be a qualified retired law enforcement officer, the officer's job prior to retiring required carrying firearm; [5]-The district court erred in ruling that the LEOSA right did not attach until the officers had in fact obtained the firearms certification.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

BCB Holdings Ltd. v. Gov't of Belize


No. 15-7063 Consolidated with 15-7069


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


650 Fed. Appx. 17; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 8914



May 13, 2016, Filed


NOTICE: PLEASE REFER TO FEDERAL RULES OF APPELLATE PROCEDURE RULE 32.1 GOVERNING THE CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gov't of Belize v. BCB Holdings Ltd., 196 L. Ed. 2d 514, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 425 (U.S., 2017)
Motion granted by, Motion denied by, Without prejudice, Injunction denied by BCB Holdings Ltd. v. Gov't of Belize, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 17070 (D.D.C., Feb. 6, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-01123).
BCB Holdings Ltd. v. Gov't of Belize, 110 F. Supp. 3d 233, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 81824 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Pursuant to the United Nations Convention on the Recognition and Enforcement of Foreign Arbitral Awards, 21 U.S.T. 2517 (1958), the New York Convention, 9 U.S.C.S. § 207, an arbitral award ordering Belize to pay two Belizean banking companies for breach of an agreement was properly upheld because there was no showing that enforcement of the award would violate U.S. notions of morality and justice, particularly with respect to the policies of the separation of powers and international comity; [2]-Although they did not seek enforcement within three years after the award was made, the action was not time-barred because the limitations period was tolled until a Belize criminal statute, that imposed imprisonment and fines on those who violated a Belize Supreme Court injunction against pursuing enforcement of arbitration awards against Belize, was declared unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed.

Holmes v. FEC


No. 15-5120


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


823 F.3d 69; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 7492


January 21, 2016, Argued 
April 26, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-01243).
Holmes v. FEC, 99 F. Supp. 3d 123, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 51414 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court properly declined to certify the voters' Fifth Amendment challenge to the Federal Election Campaign Act's contribution "asymmetry" because their claim was frivolous where it was so clearly a challenge to the regulations and not the Act, and therefore outside the scope of 52 U.S.C.S. § 30110, that it failed to raise a substantial federal question for jurisdictional purposes; [2]-The district court erred in declining to certify the voters' First Amendment claim regarding the limitations on campaign contributions because it was not "obviously frivolous" or "obviously without merit" where the constitutionality of the Act's per-election structure was by no account "settled law."


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed in part, reversed in part, and case remanded.

Williams v. Lew


No. 15-5065


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


819 F.3d 466; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 7287


February 17, 2016, Argued 
April 22, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-00183).
Williams v. Lew, 77 F. Supp. 3d 129, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 948 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A holder of federal public debt instruments lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the Debt Limit Statute, 31 U.S.C.S. § 3101. His claims of future injuries were entirely conjectural, as any future injury that he might suffer followed from an extended chain of contingencies, and he did not plausibly allege any current and ongoing injuries given that the Debt Limit Statute had been suspended; [2]-The holder's facial challenge to the Debt Limit Statute was not sufficient by itself to confer U.S. Const. art. III standing.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Gordon v. Lynch


No. 15-5113


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


817 F.3d 804; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 6175


February 5, 2016, Argued 
April 5, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-01092).
Gordon v. Holder, 85 F. Supp. 3d 78, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 38105 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court properly vacated a preliminary injunction for lack of jurisdiction because, while a Native American cigarette seller's only potential gain from continued litigation was the incremental persuasive effect of the injunction being made permanent, his lawsuit challenging enforcement of the federal Prevent All Cigarette Trafficking Act, Pub. L. 111-154 (Mar. 31, 2010), was rendered moot by events, including the seller's health problems, his deteriorating financial situation, his lack of intent to re-enter the business, the remote risk of federal prosecution or civil penalties, and any further merits decision would not shield him from the effects of possible state or local lawsuits.


OUTCOME: Vacatur affirmed.

 

35 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. Pezzat


No. 15-7040


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


818 F.3d 1; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 5965


February 8, 2016, Argued 
April 1, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-00302).
Robinson v. Pezzat, 83 F. Supp. 3d 258, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 34472 (D.D.C., 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In this 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action, if the jury believed plaintiff's testimony that the dog was lying down, it could reasonably conclude that the dog acted aggressively toward the officer only after being shot; [2]-Summary judgment was inappropriate because a jury could conclude that the officer acted unreasonably in shooting the dog; [3]-Given that the dog bit the officer hard enough to puncture her leather boots, a second officer's belief--just seconds later--that the dog continued to pose an imminent threat even absent additional aggressive behavior was hardly unreasonable; [4]-The police reports of dog shootings provided no basis for a reasonable jury to conclude that the District had notice of a pattern of likely unconstitutional conduct adequate to prove deliberate indifference.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed in part and affirmed in part.

United States v. McGill


No. 06-3190 Consolidated with 06-3193, 07-3001, 07-3003, 07-3065, 07-3124


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


815 F.3d 846; 421 U.S. App. D.C. 280; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 3734; 99 Fed. R. Evid. Serv. (Callaghan) 1069


February 2, 2015, Argued 
March 1, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Alfred v. United States, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 5107 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2017)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Oliver v. United States, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 5493 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2017)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McGill v. United States, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 5207 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 02cr00045-01), (No. 00cr00157-12), (No. 00cr00157-18), (No. 00cr00157-19), (No. 00cr00157-20), (No. 00cr00157-21).
United States v. Moore, 651 F.3d 30, 397 U.S. App. D.C. 148, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 15666 (July 29, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-On appeal by six defendants from convictions related to a drug-trafficking conspiracy, the court found no error in the district court's dismissal of a juror for misconduct during deliberations. The juror's dismissal did not violate defendant's Sixth Amendment right to conviction only by a unanimous jury because there was an independent basis for the juror's discharge under Fed. R. Crim. P. 23(b); [2]-Improper overview testimony by an FBI agent did not entitle defendants to reversal of their convictions; [3]-Some evidence of prior criminal conduct was wrongly admitted under Fed. R. Evid. 404(b). However, those admissions, while erroneous, were harmless given the overwhelming weight of admissible evidence against defendants; [4]-The prosecution's closing argument theme and statements were entirely improper and unprofessional.


OUTCOME: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, vacated in part, remanded in part.

United States v. Abney


No. 14-3074


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


812 F.3d 1079; 421 U.S. App. D.C. 135; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 1942


September 10, 2015, Argued 
February 5, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by United States v. Abney, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 20257 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 6, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cr-00191-1).
United States v. Abney, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 22998 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 13, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant's counsel's failure to seek a continuance of sentencing on defendant's conviction for possession of 68 grams of crack cocaine, which occurred five days after Congress passed the Fair Sentencing Act (FSA), Pub. L. No. 111-220, 124 Stat. 2372 (Aug. 3, 2010), and when Presidential approval was imminent, virtually assured, and actually occurred the day after sentencing, was ineffective assistance under Strickland and U.S. Const. amend. VI because it was reasonably likely that the lower mandatory minimums would have applied to defendant if he was sentenced after its enactment, and there was no strategic reason not to seek the continuance; [2]-The court explicitly declared that in the circumstances, review on appeal of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim was by de novo review.


OUTCOME: Denial of motion for reconsideration reversed; matter remanded for resentencing under FSA.

Van Hollen v. FEC


No. 15-5016 Consolidated with 15-5017


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


811 F.3d 486; 421 U.S. App. D.C. 36; 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 1005


October 20, 2015, Argued 
January 21, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Van Hollen v. FEC, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 17528 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 26, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-00766).
Van Hollen v. FEC, 74 F. Supp. 3d 407, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 164833 (D.D.C., 2014)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court erred in holding that the FEC's rule--requiring corporations and labor organizations to disclose only those donations made for the purpose of furthering electioneering communications--failed at both the Chevron Step Two and arbitrary and capricious stages because the FEC's purpose requirement was consistent with the text, history, and purposes of 52 U.S.C.S. § 30104(f) of the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act, the FEC's analytical path could reasonably be discerned from its support, burden, and privacy rationales, and the FEC exercised its unique prerogative to safeguard the First Amendment when implementing its congressional directives.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

Flytenow, Inc. v. FAA


No. 14-1168


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


808 F.3d 882; 420 U.S. App. D.C. 343; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 22050


September 25, 2015, Argued 
December 18, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Flytenow, Inc. v. FAA, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 3280 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 24, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Flytenow, Inc. v. Faa, 137 S. Ct. 618, 196 L. Ed. 2d 514, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 454 (U.S., Jan. 9, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Review of an Order of the Federal Aviation Administration.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court had jurisdiction to review the web-based service provider's petition under 49 U.S.C.S. § 46110 where the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) disclaimed any non-finality bar to review, and finality was not jurisdictional under either the APA or the Federal Aviation Act; [2]-The provider's effort to recast the common-purpose limitation as part of the definition of compensation under 14 C.F.R. § 61.113, rather than as part of an exception under which pilots were permitted to receive compensation, was rejected; [3]-Private pilots continued to enjoy the right to share expenses with their passengers, so long as they shared a common purpose and did not hold themselves out as offering services to the public; [4]-The FAA's interpretation did not violate the First Amendment or the Equal Protection Clause, and was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: Petition denied.

Jarkesy v. SEC


No. 14-5196


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


803 F.3d 9; 419 U.S. App. D.C. 394; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 17115; Fed. Sec. L. Rep. (CCH) P98,824


April 13, 2015, Argued 
September 29, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:14-cv-00114).
Jarkesy v. U.S. SEC, 48 F. Supp. 3d 32, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 78691 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where the SEC brought an administrative proceeding charging securities fraud, and where while that proceeding was ongoing, the respondent in the SEC proceeding filed suit in federal district court seeking the administrative proceeding's termination on the basis that the SEC's proceeding's initiation and conduct infringed his constitutional rights, the district court lacked jurisdiction because Congress, by establishing a detailed statutory scheme providing for an administrative proceeding before the SEC with the prospect of judicial review in a court of appeals, implicitly precluded concurrent district court jurisdiction over such a challenge.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed.

UC Health v. NLRB


No. 14-1049 Consolidated with 14-1193


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


803 F.3d 669; 419 U.S. App. D.C. 441; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 16628; 204 L.R.R.M. 3289; 166 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P10,810


February 18, 2015, Argued 
September 18, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Review and Cross-Application for Enforcement of an Order of the National Labor Relations Board.
UC Health, 2014 NLRB LEXIS 234 (Mar. 31, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-At a time when three of five members of the National Labor Relations Board improperly held office, a Regional Director (RD) of the Board had authority under 29 U.S.C.S. § 153(b) to conduct a union election and certify its result because the RD remained vested with authority on those tasks, regardless of what the Board's composition was at the time; [2]-The challenge to the composition of the Board was reviewable on appeal, despite that it was not raised before the Board pursuant to 29 U.S.C.S. § 160(e), based on case precedent that did not require the issue to have been raised earlier; [3]-As § 153(b) was silent on the issue of the RD's power to act when the Board lacked a quorum, such that it was ambiguous, the court deferred to the Board's interpretation because it was reasonable and consistent with the purpose of § 153(b).


OUTCOME: Petition for review denied.

Anderson v. Gates, 20 F. Supp. 3d 114, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 172148 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A journalist's action against the Secretary of Defense and others, seeking reversal of the termination of his embed status at a base in Afghanistan and reinstatement of his credentials and accommodation status, was properly dismissed because he did not assert a claim that was cognizable by the court; [2]-The claim was barred by the sovereign immunity of the United States, and the present matter was excluded by 5 U.S.C.S. § 701(b)(1)(G) of the Administrative Procedure Act because the events occurred within the excluded circumstances of military authority exercised in the field in time of war; [3]-Moreover, his claim was moot because the court could not put him back in the same position he occupied before the alleged events, he did not seek damages, and his alleged reputational injury was merely the "lingering effect of an otherwise moot action.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

OUTCOME: Decision affirmed.

Shelby County v. Lynch


No. 14-5138


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


799 F.3d 1173; 419 U.S. App. D.C. 139; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 15434


April 10, 2015, Argued 
September 1, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shelby County v. Lynch, 136 S. Ct. 981, 194 L. Ed. 2d 4, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 876 (U.S., Jan. 25, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00651).
Shelby County v. Holder, 43 F. Supp. 3d 47, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 72385 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A county, which prevailed in a challenge to the constitutionality of the coverage formula of § 4 of the Voting Rights Act of 1965 (VRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973b, was not entitled to attorneys' fees under the VRA's fee-shifting provision, 52 U.S.C.S. § 10310(e), because its victory did not advance any of the goals Congress meant to promote by making fees available; [2]-Under the Newman v. Piggie Park Enterprises, Inc., 390 U.S. 400, 88 S. Ct. 964, 19 L. Ed. 2d 1263 (1968), standard, which governed claims for attorneys' fees under the VRA, a party was entitled to attorneys' fees only when it showed that is success in litigation advanced the goals Congress intended to promote; [3]-The county's suit did not contribute to enforcement of the VRA, but instead, it invalidated the VRA coverage formula and immobilized a particular section of the Act.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed.

Hodge v. Talkin


No. 13-5250


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


799 F.3d 1145; 419 U.S. App. D.C. 111; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 15190


September 23, 2014, Argued 
August 28, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Hodge v. Talkin, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 19189 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 3, 2015)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Hodge v. Talkin, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 19190 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 3, 2015)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hodge v. Talkin, 136 S. Ct. 2009, 195 L. Ed. 2d 215, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 3206 (U.S., May 16, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-00104).
Hodge v. Talkin, 949 F. Supp. 2d 152, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 81727 (D.D.C., June 11, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the assemblages and display clauses of 40 U.S.C.S. § 6135, which made it unlawful to parade, stand, or move in processions or assemblages in the U.S. Supreme Court Building or grounds or to display a flag, banner, or device designed to bring into public notice a party, organization, or movement, violated the First Amendment as to the Court's plaza. HOLDINGS: [1]-The Supreme Court plaza was a nonpublic forum because its appearance and design manifested its integration with the building and its separation from surrounding areas; [2]-The statute's restrictions were reasonable in light of the government's interest in maintaining courthouse decorum and in preserving the appearance of a judiciary immune to public pressure; [3]-There were other available means to exercise First Amendment rights because § 6135 did not limit demonstrations on the adjacent public sidewalk.


OUTCOME: Decision reversed.

New York Republican State Comm. & Tenn. Republican Party v. SEC


No. 14-1194 Consolidated with 14-5242


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court properly dismissed a suit by the New York Republican State Committee and the Tennessee Republican Party (the plaintiffs) against the SEC to invalidate a four-year-old rule regulating campaign contributions by investment advisers because 15 U.S.C.S. § 80b-13(a) clearly stated that the proper course for the plaintiffs to protect their rights was to file a petition with the appellate court within 60 days of the rule's issuance, the plaintiffs' own tactical mistakes and inauspicious legal judgments did not amount to an extraordinary obstacle sufficient to warrant equitable tolling, and the review provision in § 80b-13(a) provided adequate relief for constitutional and nonconstitutional challenges to rules promulgated under the Investment Advisers Act of 1940 and, therefore, was the exclusive means for doing so.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed and petition dismissed.

May 12, 2015, Argued 
August 14, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cutler v. HHS, 136 S. Ct. 877, 193 L. Ed. 2d 719, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 372 (U.S., Jan. 11, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cv-02066).
Cutler v. United States HHS, 52 F. Supp. 3d 27, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 86279 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Plaintiff had standing to bring an Establishment Clause challenge to the religious exemption from mandatory insurance coverage under the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act, 26 U.S.C.S. § 5000A(d)(2). He alleged that he was penalized for lacking insurance just because he was not religiously motivated; [2]-The Establishment Clause claim failed on the merits because the exemption was an accommodation that was confined to alleviating government-created burdens on private religious exercise; [3]-Plaintiff lacked standing to bring an equal protection claim challenging a transitional policy allowing states to permit the issuance of non-compliant insurance plans for an interim period. His alleged injury was the result of his private insurer's cancellation of his insurance, not the government's transitional policy.


OUTCOME: Dismissal affirmed.

Arpaio v. Obama

UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

May 4, 2015, Argued

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A county sheriff lacked standing to challenge government policies to temporarily defer removal of non-dangerous aliens since it was unduly speculative that a predicted and protracted chain of events would increase the county's expenditures of resources on investigation, apprehension, and incarceration of criminals, and thus there was no showing of causation between the policies and increased law enforcement expenditures; [2]-The sheriff also failed to show injury from aliens under deferred removal who would remain in the county and might commit crimes, since the deferred removal policies applied only to non-dangerous aliens and the government lacked the resources to promptly remove all removable aliens even without the deferral policies.

Fla. Bankers Ass'n v. United States Dep't of Treasury, 19 F. Supp. 3d 111, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 3521 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because the Tax Code defined the penalty as a tax, a tax was imposed as a direct consequence of violating the regulation, and invalidating the regulation would directly bar collection of that tax; [2]-If the bankers associations' challenge were successful, the IRS would be unable to assess or collect that tax for failure to comply with the reporting requirement, and invalidating the reporting requirement would necessarily restrain the assessment and collection of the tax, which the appellate court could not do; [3]-The bankers associations' challenge to the reporting requirements in 26 C.F.R. §§ 1.6049-4 and 1.6049-8 was barred by the Anti-Injunction Act and the tax exception to the Declaratory Judgment Act.


OUTCOME: Judgment vacated and remanded.

Doe v. District of Columbia

UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

November 14, 2014, Argued 
August 11, 2015, Decided

Doe v. District of Columbia, 958 F. Supp. 2d 178, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 108501 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Given the uncertainty regarding when exactly an exigency exists and lack of the court's own controlling precedent, the law in question was not "clearly established" at the time of the seizure of the children. Individual defendants were thus entitled to qualified immunity as held below; [2]-Regarding municipal liability, because the constitutional question was difficult, and there was a question whether the District was liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, the district court should address the question of municipal liability in the first instance. Further, the court remanded the question of what relevance, if any, a policy had on the District's liability; [3]-Accordingly, it vacated the grant of summary judgment on Fourth and Fifth Amendment claims against the District and remanded for the district court to determine whether municipal liability was permissible under Monell.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the grant of summary judgment on the Fourth and Fifth Amendment claims against the District and remanded for further proceedings. The court affirmed the judgment of the district court on the remaining claims.

OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Am. Coal Co. v. Fed. Mine Safety & Health Review Comm'n


No. 14-1206


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


796 F.3d 18; 418 U.S. App. D.C. 18; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 13340


May 5, 2015, Argued 
July 31, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Review of a Decision of the Federal Mine Safety & Health Review Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Term "fire" (30 U.S.C.S. § 802(k)) as used in the Federal Mine Safety and Health Act of 1977 ("Mine Act"), Pub. L. No. 95-164, 91 Stat. 1290 (codified as amended at 30 U.S.C.S. § 801 et seq.), is ambiguous because there are competing, plausible ways to read the term; [2]-The Secretary of Labor reasonably determined that the term did not require the presence of flames, and substantial evidence supported the conclusion that the smoldering patches on the coal company's stockpile satisfied the Secretary's interpretation of a "fire"; [3]-A smoldering fire poses active risks and can constitute an "accident occurring" on a coal stockpile in its own right, 30 U.S.C.S. § 813(k); [4]-Substantial evidence supported the Federal Mine Safety and Health Review Commission's determination that the smoldering patches reasonably had the potential to burst into flame.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was denied.

Combat Veterans for Congress Political Comm. v. FEC


No. 13-5358


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


795 F.3d 151; 417 U.S. App. D.C. 414; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 13036


February 5, 2015, Argued 
July 28, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Combat Veterans for Cong. PAC v. FEC, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 18025 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 16, 2015)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Combat Veterans for Cong. PAC v. FEC, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 18023 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 16, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-02168).
Combat Veterans for Congress PAC v. FEC, 983 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 140450 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Even if the FEC's use of its 24-hour, no-objection procedure was in error, a political committee failed to show any likelihood that any material FEC action or decision would have been different with respect to missed deadlines had a tally voting procedure been used for the reason-to-believe decisions, and thus, any error was harmless; [2]-The use of the no-objection procedure was harmless where the political committee failed to explain how it was prejudiced, there was no hint the FEC would have decided differently, and the FEC's ratification of a defect in a reason-to-believe finding by a subsequent, valid tally vote remedied the earlier error; [3]-When a staff member, acting on a Commissioner's instructions, signed a dispositive number of the ballots the individual Commissioners submitted to ratify the FEC's ultimate determination, such a ballot was validly cast.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed.

State Nat'l Bank of Big Spring v. Lew


No. 13-5247 Consolidated with 13-5248


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


795 F.3d 48; 417 U.S. App. D.C. 311; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 12785


November 19, 2014, Argued 
July 24, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-01032).
State Nat'l Bank of Big Spring v. Lew, 958 F. Supp. 2d 127, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 108308 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The circuit court erred in finding that a bank lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau because the bank could challenge the director's recess appointment and it was regulated by the Bureau under 12 U.S.C.S. § 5491(a); [2]-The circuit court properly found that the bank lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the Financial Stability Oversight Council because it was not subject to additional regulation under 12 U.S.C.S. §§ 5323(a)(1), 5365 and the extra regulatory burden on a competitor actually created a reputational benefit for it; [3]-The circuit court properly found that the states lacked standing to challenge the Government's orderly liquidation authority under 12 U.S.C.S. §§ 5384, 5390 because their current investments were not worth less or adversely affected by the authority.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.

Bailey v. Fulwood


No. 13-5177


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


793 F.3d 127; 417 U.S. App. D.C. 127; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 12076


February 12, 2015, Argued 
July 14, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bailey v. Smoot, 196 L. Ed. 2d 27, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 4983 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-00498).
Bailey v. Fulwood, 945 F. Supp. 2d 62, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 70752 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Former D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 28, § 100 et seq. (1987) and a 1991 Policy Guideline did not constrain the discretion of the U.S. Parole Commission (USPC), and the USPC therefore did not violate either of them when it denied a prisoner parole after finding a reasonable probability that he would not obey the law if released and that his release would endanger the public safety; [2]-There was no Ex Post Facto Clause violation because the government did not rely on the retroactive application of any statute, regulation, or guideline to justify its denial of the prisoner's parole requests. It was apparent from the record that the USPC applied the 1987 regulations, which were in effect when the prisoner committed the underlying criminal offense.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Pollack v. Duff


No. 13-5263


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


793 F.3d 34; 417 U.S. App. D.C. 34; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 11622


January 9, 2015, Argued 
July 7, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00866).
Pollack v. Duff, 958 F. Supp. 2d 280, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 110082 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The decision of the Administrative Office of the U.S. Courts to limit its applicant pool to federal judiciary employees, regardless of where they lived, or to persons who lived in the Washington metropolitan area did not violate appellant's right to travel under the Privileges and Immunities Clause of U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1, as the Clause did not apply to actions taken by branches of the federal government; [2]-The geographical limitation did not implicate appellant's right to travel protected by the equal protection component of the Due Process Clause, as it did not penalize the exercise of her right to travel interstate, but gave her an incentive to do so; [3]-The trial court did not err by denying her request for discovery before entering summary judgment against her, as she did not show why the facts she intended to discover were necessary to the litigation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re Al-Nashiri


No. 14-1203


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


791 F.3d 71; 416 U.S. App. D.C. 248; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 10549


February 10, 2015, Argued 
June 23, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Writ of Mandamus and Prohibition to the United States Court of Military Commission Review.
United States v. Al-Nashiri, 62 F. Supp. 3d 1305, 2014 Military Commission Review 2 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A detainee's petition for a writ of mandamus and prohibition disqualifying military judges on his United States Court of Military Commission Review (CMCR) panel was denied because the detainee could adequately raise his constitutional challenges on direct appeal from final judgment; [2]-The Military Commissions Act of 2009, 10 U.S.C.S. § 950g(a) and (d), empowered the court of appeals to review all matters of law once a military commission issued a final judgment and both the convening authority and the CMCR reviewed it, and that provision would allow the court of appeals to consider the detainee's constitutional challenges on direct appeal; [3]-The detainee failed to identify some irreparable" injury that would go unredressed if he did not secure mandamus relief because he did not allege that the military judges on the CMCR were biased against him in fact or apparently.


OUTCOME: Petition denied.

Z St. v. Koskinen


No. 15-5010


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


791 F.3d 24; 416 U.S. App. D.C. 201; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 10326; 2015-1 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50,345; 115 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 2190


May 4, 2015, Argued 
June 19, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-00401).
Z St., Inc. v. Koskinen, 44 F. Supp. 3d 48, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 71638 (D.D.C., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where applicant for 26 U.S.C.S. § 501(c)(3) tax exemption filed a declaratory judgment action to prevent the IRS from delaying consideration of its application in violation of the First Amendment, the Anti-Injunction Act, 26 U.S.C.S. § 7421, did not bar the action because the applicant sought not to restrain the assessment or collection of a tax, but rather to obtain relief from unconstitutional delay; [2]-Although 26 U.S.C.S. § 7428 provided a remedy, that remedy could not address the applicant's alleged injury; [3]-Neither 26 U.S.C.S. § 6213 nor § 7422 allowed the court to review the allegedly unconstitutional delay; [4]-Sovereign immunity presented no bar to the suit because 5 U.S.C.S. § 702 waived sovereign immunity with respect to suits for nonmonetary damages that alleged wrongful action by an agency or its officers or employees.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Ali Hamza Ahmad Suliman Al Bahlul v. United States


No. 11-1324


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


792 F.3d 1; 416 U.S. App. D.C. 340; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 9868


October 22, 2014, Argued 
June 12, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Rehearing granted by, En banc Bahlul v. United States, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 16967 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 25, 2015)
Different results reached on reconsideration by, En banc Ali Hamza Ahmad Suliman Al Bahlul v. United States, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 18852 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 20, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Review from the United States Court of Military Commission Review.
United States v. Al Bahlul, 820 F. Supp. 2d 1141, 2011 Military Commission Review 3 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-An alleged terrorist did not forfeit a challenge to a conviction for inchoate conspiracy by a law of war military commission by failing to raise the issue in the commission proceedings, since the terrorist's structural claim of executive encroachment on the judicial power raised significant separation of powers concerns which was not subject to forfeiture; [2]-The terrorist was improperly convicted of conspiracy by the law of war military commission since the U.S. Const. art. III exception from judicial power for the commission was limited to international law of war offenses which did not entail a right to trial by jury at common law, conspiracy was not a violation of the international law of war, and thus the executive commission intruded upon the constitutional judicial power.


OUTCOME: Conviction vacated.
