Miss. Comm'n on Envtl. Quality v. EPA


No. 12-1309 Consolidated with 12-1310, 12-1312, 12-1313, 12-1315, 12-1316, 12-1317, 12-1318, 12-1322, 12-1323, 12-1326, 12-1328, 13-1030, 13-1032, 13-1046, 13-1050, 13-1051, 13-1052, 13-1053, 13-1054


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


790 F.3d 138; 416 U.S. App. D.C. 69; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 9115; 45 ELR 20104; 80 ERC (BNA) 1861


October 21, 2014, Argued 
June 2, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petitions for Review of Final Action of the United States Environmental Protection Agency.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The EPA's final nonattainment designations of Delaware and Connecticut counties were consistent with a reasonable interpretation of the Clean Air Act; [2]-The EPA's designation of Uinta Basin, Utah, as "unclassifiable" was rational and in accordance with the Clean Air Act. The EPA reasonably explained that private monitoring data afforded an insufficient basis for a nonattainment designation because the agency was unable to perform post-collection quality assurance checks on the data; [3]-The Clean Air Act did not violate the Tenth Amendment or exceed Congress' authority under the Spending Clause because the Clean Air Act did not compel states to enact federal regulatory programs. Instead, the statutory scheme authorized the EPA to promulgate and administer a federal implementation plan of its own if a state failed to submit an adequate state implementation plan.


OUTCOME: The consolidated petitions for review were denied.

Abdelfattah v. United States Dep't of Homeland Sec.


No. 12-5322


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


787 F.3d 524; 415 U.S. App. D.C. 275; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 8010


December 4, 2014, Argued 
May 15, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Abdelfattah v. United States Dep't of Homeland Sec., 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 11694 (D.C. Cir., July 7, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cv-01842).
Osama Abdelfattah v. United States Dep't of Homeland Sec., 893 F. Supp. 2d 75, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 138850 (D.D.C., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court properly dismissed claims by a lawful permanent resident under the Privacy Act of 1974, 5 U.S.C.S. § 552a, arising from an agency's collection and maintenance of the Treasury Enforcement Communication System records, because although the equitable relief of expungement of the records was available and the issue was not moot, he failed to state a claim that was actionable; [2]-The resident's claim under the Right to Financial Privacy Act, 12 U.S.C.S. § 3401 et seq., failed because the facts alleged did not show a violation within the Act's narrow provisions, including that he was a customer, what was the source of the alleged disclosure, and whether the source was a "financial institution"; [3]-Claims under the Fair Credit Reporting Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 6801 et seq., should have survived because allegations involved a "consumer report."


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed except as to claims under Fair Credit Reporting Act.

Morgan Drexen, Inc. v. Consumer Fin. Prot. Bureau


No. 13-5342


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


785 F.3d 684; 415 U.S. App. D.C. 85; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 7229


November 19, 2014, Argued 
May 1, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Morgan Drexen, Inc. v. Consumer Fin. Prot. Bureau, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 12768 (D.C. Cir., July 23, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cv-01112).
Consumer Fin. Prot. Bureau v. Morgan Drexen, Inc., 60 F. Supp. 3d 1082, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 158765 (C.D. Cal., Jan. 10, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Attorney failed to proffer evidence in support of any of her theories of standing: that she was responsible for allegedly illegal conduct of the other appellant (a business), that her practice was or would be economically harmed by the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau's enforcement action against a certain business, or that implicit accusations by the Bureau that she exercised too little control over the business or engaged in illegal conduct herself could damage her professional standing. The attorney, who contracted with the business for paralegal services, lacked standing under U.S. Const. art. III; [2]-The business, which sought a permanent injunction, failed to show that the district court abused its discretion in concluding that it had an adequate remedy at law in the California enforcement action and that it suffered no irreparable injury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint.

 

79 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Delta Constr. Co. v. EPA


No. 11-1428, Consolidated with 11-1441, 12-1427, No. 13-1076


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


783 F.3d 1291; 414 U.S. App. D.C. 428; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 6780; 45 ELR 20081; 80 ERC (BNA) 1341


January 9, 2015, Argued 
April 24, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, Rehearing, en banc, denied by Delta Constr. Co. v. EPA, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 13602 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 3, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petitions for Review of Final Agency Actions of the Environmental Protection Agency and the Department of Transportation. On Petition for Review of Final Agency Action of the United States Environmental Protection Agency.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Purchasers of new vehicles lacked standing to challenge greenhouse gas emission standards under a joint rule also governing fuel economy standards by alleging harm from the improper promulgation of the emissions standards, since the emission and fuel economy standards were essentially the same and thus the purchasers could not show that the emission standards alone caused the harm from increased vehicle prices or that the harm could be redressed by a favorable decision; [2]-While a purveyor of clean fuel had competitor standing to challenge a decision not to incentivize the purchase of its product, the purveyor's interest was not within the zone of interests of the regulation governing emissions standards for motor vehicles, even though the purveyor's financial interests currently appeared to align with the goals of achieving clean air.


OUTCOME: Petitions for review dismissed.

Abbas v. Foreign Policy Group, LLC


No. 13-7171k


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


783 F.3d 1328; 414 U.S. App. D.C. 465; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 6782; 43 Media L. Rep. 1841; 91 Fed. R. Serv. 3d (Callaghan) 544


October 20, 2014, Argued 
April 24, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-01565).
Abbas v. Foreign Policy Group, LLC, 975 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 139177 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-As Fed. R. Civ. P. 12 and 56 answered the same question as the D.C. Anti-Strategic Lawsuits Against Public Participation Act (Anti-SLAPP), and Rules 12 and 56 were valid under the Rules Enabling Act, 28 U.S.C.S. § 2072, a federal court exercising diversity jurisdiction was required to apply Rules 12 and 56 instead of the Anti-SLAPP Act's special motion to dismiss provision, D.C. Code § 16-5502(a); [2]-However, dismissal of a foreign political leader's son's defamation action against a website publisher and the article's author was proper because under Fed. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6), the allegations did not suffice to make out a defamation claim where they were merely questions, which were not factual representations.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

United States v. Munoz Miranda


No. 13-3032 Consolidated with 13-3036


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


780 F.3d 1185; 414 U.S. App. D.C. 305; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 4534; 2015 AMC 1003


September 15, 2014, Argued 
March 20, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cr-00106-5), (No. 1:10-cr-00106-2).
United States v. Carvajal, 924 F. Supp. 2d 219, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 22861 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendants' unconditional guilty pleas waived their right to argue that the MDLEA was unconstitutional as applied to their conduct because their drug conspiracy did not take place on the high seas and because application of the MDLEA violated their due process rights. The claims did not fall within the exception to waiver for issues that implicated subject matter jurisdiction; [2]-Defendants' claim that vessels they used to transport drugs were not subject to the jurisdiction of the United States under the MDLEA, 46 U.S.C.S. § 70504(a), concerned the district court's subject matter jurisdiction and therefore could not be waived; [3]-The district court did not err in finding that the vessels were subject to the jurisdiction of the United States pursuant to 46 U.S.C.S. § 70502(c)(1)(A) because they were vessels without nationality.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

82 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Fenwick v. Pudimott


No. 13-5130


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


778 F.3d 133; 414 U.S. App. D.C. 179; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 2264


December 9, 2014, Argued 
February 13, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Fenwick v. United States, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 103686 (D.D.C., Aug. 7, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cv-02330).
Fenwick v. United States, 926 F. Supp. 2d 201, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 28720 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where officers surrounded a driver's car with guns drawn, ordered him to halt, and shot him when he clipped an officer with the car's side mirror, the officers were entitled to qualified immunity as to the driver's excessive force claim because it was not clearly established that the officers violated the Fourth Amendment by using deadly force to prevent his flight since he operated his car in a way that endangered an officer because, under Heck, the Superior Court, in finding that the driver committed felony assault, determined that moments before the shooting, his driving had posed a "grave risk of causing significant bodily injury" to an officer under D.C. Code § 22-405(c).


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and case remanded.

City of New York v. AMTRAK


No. 13-7119


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


776 F.3d 11; 414 U.S. App. D.C. 11; 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 688


November 24, 2014, Argued 
January 16, 2015, Decided


NOTICE:

THIS OPINION IS SUBJECT TO FORMAL REVISION BEFORE PUBLICATION IN THE ATLANTIC AND MARYLAND REPORTERS. USERS ARE REQUESTED TO NOTIFY THE CLERK OF THE COURT OF ANY FORMAL ERRORS SO THAT CORRECTIONS MAY BE MADE BEFORE THE BOUND VOLUMES GO TO PRESS.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-01169).
City of New York v. Amtrak, 960 F. Supp. 2d 84, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 103979 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a provision in a deed of land to Amtrak's predecessor, which required the predecessor to maintain a bridge owned by the City of New York, was enforceable after conveyance of the land to Amtrak under the Regional Rail Reorganization Act of 1973. HOLDINGS: [1]-45 U.S.C.S. § 743(b)(2), which provided for conveyance of rail property free and clear of any liens or encumbrances, did not provide the same protection for a holder of a property interest as the Bankruptcy Code; [2]-Section 743(b)(2) was not rendered unconstitutional by allowing extinction of property claims that would allegedly survive bankruptcy, as the Rail Act was not passed solely pursuant to Congress's bankruptcy authority; [3]-The court of appeals declined to review a claim under the public utility doctrine, as the City failed to file a Fed. R. Civ. P. 59(e) or 60(b)(6) motion in district court.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Priests for Life v. United States HHS

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Priests for Life v. United States HHS, 808 F.3d 1, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 8326 (D.C. Cir., 2015)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, Motion granted by Priests for Life v. HHS, 136 S. Ct. 446, 193 L. Ed. 2d 345, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 7038 (U.S., 2015)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, Motion granted by Roman Catholic Archbishop v. Burwell, 136 S. Ct. 444, 193 L. Ed. 2d 345, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 7032 (U.S., 2015)
Later proceeding at Roman Catholic Archbishop v. Burwell, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 2269 (U.S., Mar. 29, 2016)

Priests for Life v. United States HHS, 7 F. Supp. 3d 88, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 177951 (D.D.C., 2013)

OUTCOME: Affirmed in part; vacated in part.

Gross v. United States


No. 13-5168


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


771 F.3d 10; 413 U.S. App. D.C. 125; 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 21623


September 19, 2014, Argued 
November 14, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gross v. United States, 135 S. Ct. 1746, 191 L. Ed. 2d 703, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 2433 (U.S., Apr. 6, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:12-cv-01860).
Gross v. Dev. Alternatives, Inc., 946 F. Supp. 2d 120, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 74638 (D.D.C., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The foreign country exception in 28 U.S.C.S. § 2680(k) to the waiver of sovereign immunity from tort claims in 28 U.S.C.S. § 1346(b)(1) of the Federal Tort Claims Act applied to bar claims of economic injuries arising from an individual's imprisonment in a foreign country, which was a consequence of working on a federal government contract to promote democracy, because the economic injuries alleged to have occurred in the United States were derivative of the injuries that occurred abroad; [2]-An equal protection argument lacked merit because there was a rational basis for distinguishing between claimants injured abroad and those injured in the United States, which was to insulate the federal government from tort liability imposed pursuant to foreign law, and the district court was not required to determine whether or not foreign law would control in a particular case.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Wesby v. District of Columbia


No. 12-7127


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


765 F.3d 13; 412 U.S. App. D.C. 246; 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 16893


March 27, 2014, Argued 
September 2, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Wesby v. District of Columbia, 816 F.3d 96, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 2140 (D.C. Cir., 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, Motion granted by District of Columbia v. Wesby, 2017 U.S. LEXIS 788 (U.S., Jan. 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-00501).
Wesby v. District of Columbia, 841 F. Supp. 2d 20, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 5680 (D.D.C., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where arrestees were invited to a house for a party by a woman that they reasonably believed to be the house's lawful occupant, even though the putative host had not finalized any rental agreement, summary judgment was properly granted in favor of arrestees in their § 1983 unlawful arrest suit because officers lacked Fourth Amendment probable cause for the arrests for unlawful entry since the arrestees reasonably believed that the host resided at the house; [2]-The officers also lacked probable cause to arrest for disorderly conduct because the evidence did not show any disturbance of sufficient magnitude to violate local laws, particularly as the arrests were based on the complaining phone call of only one neighbor; [3]-As a supervising police sergeant at the scene overstepped clear law in directing the arrests, the government was liable for negligent supervision.


OUTCOME: Decision affirmed.

In re Charges of Judicial Misconduct

UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

August 12, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 05-13-90099 (Fifth Circuit).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The complaint of judicial conduct against an appellate court judge filed pursuant to 28 U.S.C.S. § 351(a) was dismissed because although the judge admitted to making comments that were disrespectful to a fellow circuit judge, the judge had already apologized and the circuit judge had accepted that apology, and the judge's remarks during a law school lecture did not exhibit bias toward certain classes of claimants and claims, inappropriately criticize the Justice Department and United States Supreme Court, or display a lack of appropriate judicial demeanor so as to violate the misconduct rules.

Schnitzler v. United States


No. 12-5200


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


761 F.3d 33; 411 U.S. App. D.C. 412; 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 15272


April 21, 2014, Argued 
August 8, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-01318).
Schnitzler v. United States, 863 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 72909 (D.D.C., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The district court erred by dismissing a suit brought by a South Dakota state prisoner who wanted to renounce his United States citizenship under 8 U.S.C.S. § 1481(a) as the prisoner had standing because he remained a citizen against his wishes and allegedly in violation of his constitutional rights; [2]-The district court erred by finding that a letter from the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services, informing the prisoner that it would hold his application in abeyance pending an in-person interview, rendered the complaint moot; [3]-A judicial determination that the agency's interview policy was unconstitutional could redress his injury; [4]-The prisoner had standing to pursue his equal protection claims, alleging that in-person interview requirement posed a barrier that discriminated against those who could not travel to renounce their citizenship.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

Stop This Insanity Inc. Employee Leadership Fund v. FEC

UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

November 19, 2013, Argued 
August 5, 2014, Decided

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The corporation's decision to form the segregated campaign fund, 2 U.S.C.S. § 441b, was purely voluntary, and the statutory scheme did not compel the corporation to form the segregated fund, lest it be without a vehicle for political speech in the form of independent expenditures; [2]-The restrictions were not properly treated as constraints on independent expenditures as they did not restrict the amount or manner in which a political entity could spend money, and did not truly silence the segregated fund as the speaker; [3]-The corporation made no attempt to refute the legitimacy of the interest invoked or examine how closely the restrictions on the segregated fund hewed to the interest.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Am. Meat Inst. v. United States Dep't of Agric.


No. 13-5281


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


760 F.3d 18; 411 U.S. App. D.C. 318; 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 14398; 36 Int'l Trade Rep. (BNA) 483; 44 ELR 20173


May 19, 2014, Argued 
July 29, 2014, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by, Petition denied by Am. Meat Inst. v. United States Dep't of Agric., 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 20939 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 31, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:13-cv-01033).
Am. Meat Inst. v. United States Dep't of Agric., 968 F. Supp. 2d 38, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 129099 (D.D.C., 2013)
Am. Meat Inst. v. United States Dep't of Agric., 746 F.3d 1065, 409 U.S. App. D.C. 96, 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 5710 (D.C. Cir., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Under the First Amendment, government interests in addition to correcting deception can be invoked to sustain a disclosure mandate of purely factual and uncontroversial information about the terms under which products or services are available; [2]-The government had a substantial interest in mandating disclosure of country-of-origin information about meat products, based on a long history of such disclosures to enable consumers to choose American-made products, demonstrated consumer interest in extending country-of-origin labeling to food products, and the individual health concerns and market impacts that can arise in the event of a food-borne illness outbreak; [3]-Means-end fit is self-evidently satisfied when the government acts only through a reasonably crafted mandate to disclose purely factual and uncontroversial information about attributes of a product or service.


OUTCOME: Judgment reinstated.

Ali Hamza Ahmad Suliman Al Bahlul v. United States


No. 11-1324


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


767 F.3d 1; 412 U.S. App. D.C. 372; 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 13287


September 30, 2013, Argued 
July 14, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: United States v. Al Bahlul, 820 F. Supp. 2d 1141, 2011 Military Commission Review 3 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where petitioner refused to participate in military commission proceedings, he forfeited constitutional arguments such that his convictions could be reviewed only for plain error; [2]-The Military Commissions Act of 2006 unambiguously authorized petitioner's prosecution for charged offenses based on pre-2006 conduct; [3]-Petitioner's ex post facto challenge to his conspiracy conviction was rejected because the conduct was already criminalized under 18 U.S.C.S. § 2332(b) when petitioner engaged in it, and it was not "plain" that conspiracy was not already triable by law-of-war military commission under 10 U.S.C.S. § 821 when petitioner's conduct occurred; [4]-It was, however, a plain ex post facto violation to try petitioner by military commission for providing material support for terrorism and soliciting others to commit war crimes.


OUTCOME: Ex post facto challenge to conspiracy conviction rejected, but conviction remanded for review of remaining issues; Material support and solicitation convictions vacated and remanded to determine the effect, if any, of the two vacaturs on sentencing.

Edwards v. District of Columbia


No. 13-7063 Consolidated with 13-7064


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


755 F.3d 996; 410 U.S. App. D.C. 354; 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 12114


May 5, 2014, Argued 
June 27, 2014, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-01557).
Edwards v. District of Columbia, 943 F. Supp. 2d 109, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 64836 (D.D.C., 2013)
Edwards v. District of Columbia, 765 F. Supp. 2d 3, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 18552 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-For purposes of D.C. Code § 47-2836, regulations defining a tour guide, D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 19 § 1200.1, imposing an exam requirement and related fees, D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 19, § 1203.3, and providing a tour-bus driver exemption, D.C. Mun. Regs. tit. 19, § 1204.3, are unconstitutional facially and as-applied under U.S. Const. amend. I because there is no evidence that ill-informed guides are indeed a problem for the District of Columbia's tourism industry, there is no evidence the exam regulation actually furthers the District's interest in preventing the stated harms, and the tour-bus exemption is arbitrary and renders the regulations impermissibly underinclusive.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded.

CASE SUMMARY:

111 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Zivotofsky v. Sec'y of State


No. 07-5347


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


725 F.3d 197; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 324; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 14894


March 19, 2013, Argued 
July 23, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Zivotofsky v. Kerry, 134 S. Ct. 1873, 188 L. Ed. 2d 910, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 2885 (U.S., 2014)
Affirmed by Zivotofsky v. Kerry, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 3781 (U.S., June 8, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 03cv01921).
Zivotofsky v. Sec'y of State, 511 F. Supp. 2d 97, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 68849 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a United States citizen born in Jerusalem, sought an injunction ordering appellee, the Secretary of the U.S. Department of State, to issue him a passport listing "Israel" as his place of birth in accordance with § 214(d) of the Foreign Relations Authorization Act, Fiscal Year 2003, Pub. L. No. 107-228, 116 Stat. 1350. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the complaint.


OVERVIEW: Section 214(d) of the Foreign Relations Authorization Act required the Secretary to record "Israel" as the place of birth on the passport of a United States citizen born in Jerusalem if the citizen so requested. The court held that § 214(d) was unconstitutional because it impermissibly intruded on the President's exclusive authority to decide whether and on what terms to recognize foreign nations. Section 214(d) conflicted with the long-standing Executive branch policy of neutrality on what nation or government, if any, was sovereign over Jerusalem. U.S. Presidents had consistently endeavored not to engage in official actions that would recognize, or might be perceived as constituting recognition of, Jerusalem as either the capital city of Israel or as a city located within the sovereign territory of Israel. By attempting to alter the State Department's treatment of passport applicants born in Jerusalem, § 214(d) directly contradicted a carefully considered exercise of the Executive branch's recognition power. The Executive branch's belief that § 214(d) would cause adverse foreign policy consequences was conclusive on the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment dismissing the complaint.

 

112 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Mississippi v. EPA


No. 08-1200 Consolidated with 08-1202, 08-1203, 08-1204, 08-1206


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


723 F.3d 246; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 223; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 14885; 43 ELR 20158


November 16, 2012, Argued 
July 23, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reprinted as amended at Mississippi v. EPA, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 24596 (D.C. Cir., July 23, 2013)
Request granted, Costs and fees proceeding at Mississippi v. EPA, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 25835 (D.C. Cir., Dec. 17, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of a Final Rule Issued by the United States Environmental Protection Agency.
Mississippi v. EPA, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 3313 (D.C. Cir., Feb. 17, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court considered several challenges to respondent Environmental Protection Agency's (EPA's) most recent revisions to the primary and secondary National Ambient Air Quality Standards (NAAQS) for ozone. Petitioner was the State of Mississippi.


OVERVIEW: The State of Mississippi and industry petitioners (collectively "Mississippi) challenged EPA's threshold decision to revise the primary NAAQS level. According to Mississippi, several aspects of EPA's decision were arbitrary, including its allegedly unsupported finding that the revised NAAQS would provide increased protection; its failure to compare the 2008 risk assessment with the 1997 risk assessment; and the allegedly inadequate and distorted science on which the agency relied. The court rejected these arguments. Next, it noted that the governmental and environmental petitioners argued that the NAAQS was too lax. The Clean Air Act required the court to overturn any EPA action that was arbitrary, capricious, an abuse of discretion, or contrary to law, 42 U.S.C.S. § 7607(d)(9)(A). EPA's treatment of the evidence satisfied the court's deferential standard of review. Finally, the court found that EPA's justification for the secondary standard was inadequate under American Farm Bureau. Because EPA failed to determine what level of protection was "requisite to protect the public welfare," 42 U.S.C.S. § 7409(b)(2), its explanation for the secondary standard violated the Clean Air Act.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the secondary NAAQS to EPA for reconsideration. In all other respects, the petitions for review were denied.

 

113 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Kourouma v. FERC


No. 11-1283


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


723 F.3d 274; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 251; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 14881


December 13, 2012, Argued 
July 23, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Federal Energy Regulatory Commission (FERC) ordered petitioner energy trader to pay a $50,000 civil penalty because he had made false statements and material omissions in forms he filed with FERC and a market operator FERC regulated. The trader appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trader argued that he was entitled to an administrative hearing under 16 U.S.C.S. § 823b(d)(2)(A). The appellate court noted that no evidentiary hearing was needed. The trader's affidavit made the violation clear. He admitted that he falsified and omitted multiple names on his forms, and that he had kept his involvement in a company a secret to avoid alerting his employer to his violation of the non-compete. The trader's admissions resolved all disputes of material fact, making an evidentiary hearing unnecessary. Because the trader's actions were worse than careless, FERC reasonably concluded that he violated Market Behavior Rule 3. FERC's goal was to ensure that inadvertent false submissions would not be penalized. The trader's false filings were not inadvertent. FERC followed its own summary disposition rule concerning evidence. The trader was not permitted to introduce new evidence, 18 C.F.R. § 385.213(a)(2). FERC's decision to impose a $50,000 penalty was rationally supported by multiple pieces of evidence. The penalty was not increased to promote general deterrence.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was denied.

Mississippi v. EPA

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A challenge to a revision of the primary National Ambient Air Quality Standards (NAAQS) was rejected as the EPA reasonably explained how the scientific evidence had changed, and the available evidence supported the decision; [2]-A challenge to the level of the primary ozone NAAQS was rejected as the EPA was not required to rely on statistically uncertain results, its discussion of the limitations of epidemiological studies was rational, and its cautious treatment of risk and exposure assessments was proper; [3]-The EPA's judgment that the primary ozone NAAQS was an adequate margin of safety under 42 U.S.C.S. § 7409(b)(1) was upheld; [4]-A decision to set identical secondary ozone NAAQS violated 42 U.S.C.S. § 7409(b)(2) where the EPA had only compared the revised primary standard to a seasonal level that it had never specified was requisite to protect the public welfare.

115 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Taylor v. Huerta


No. 12-1140


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


723 F.3d 210; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 187; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 14620


February 12, 2013, Argued 
July 19, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Request granted, Costs and fees proceeding at Taylor v. Huerta, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 21099 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 18, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the National Transportation Safety Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner's pilot and medical certificates were revoked because petitioner, a pilot, had falsely stated that he had never been arrested for drunk driving. An administrative law judge (ALJ) upheld the revocation order, and the National Transportation Safety Board (NTSB) affirmed. The pilot sought review.


OVERVIEW: The pilot submitted an application for a medical certificate using the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA) online system. He pressed the "no" button for a series of questions, including one that asked whether he had any arrests or convictions involving driving while intoxicated. The pilot had been arrested, but not convicted, for drunk driving. The FAA revoked his certificate and the pilot appealed. The pilot testified that he had not read the question carefully to understand that arrests without convictions were included. The ALJ concluded that the pilot had violated 14 C.F.R. § 67.403(a)(1) because he intentionally failed to carefully read the question. On review, the court affirmed because the case was indistinguishable from a similar case where the court had affirmed application of the regulation in the same way. While the pilot argued that the online application entrapped him into not reading all the questions carefully, the court reaffirmed that an intentional failure to carefully read the questions before submitting answers was sufficient to meet the regulation's scienter requirement. The FAA's decision to provide a convenience did not "entrap" applicants.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for review.

 

116 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Ctr. for Biological Diversity v. EPA


No. 11-1101 Consolidated with 11-1285, 11-1328, 11-1336


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


722 F.3d 401; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 140; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 14108; 43 ELR 20157; 76 ERC (BNA) 2210


April 8, 2013, Argued 
July 12, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, Motion dismissed by, As moot Ctr. for Biological Diversity v. EPA, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 12908 (D.C. Cir., July 24, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of Administrative Action of the Environmental Protection Agency.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) issued a "Deferral Rule" deferring regulation of "biogenic" carbon dioxide (BCD) for 3 years, citing scientific uncertainty over how to account for BCD's unique role in the carbon cycle, and justifying the Rule on the basis of the one-step-at-a-time and administrative necessity doctrines. Petitioner environmental groups sought review.


OVERVIEW: Under the Rule, BCD sources with a potential to emit over certain thresholds did not require a permit, while "anyway" sources (those that required a permit anyway due to conventional pollutants) still had to install the best available control technology (BACT) for BCD emissions. The EPA's invocation of the one-step-at-a-time doctrine was arbitrary and capricious because the Rule failed to offer an interpretation of the Clean Air Act that allowed treating BCD sources differently, and not merely due to scientific uncertainty. A middle-ground option, requiring permits only if a BCD source failed to try to account for net carbon cycle impacts, would have reduced emissions from sources the Rule permanently exempted. Rejecting that option without explanation was arbitrary and capricious, given that despite scientific uncertainty about measuring BCD emissions at the Prevention of Significant Deterioration of Air Quality applicability stage, the unique characteristics of those sources could be factored in at the BACT stage. The absurd results doctrine was mentioned only in passing references that did not satisfy the fundamental obligation to set forth reasons.


OUTCOME: The petition was granted and the Deferral Rule was vacated.

Ass'n of Am. R.R. v. United States DOT


No. 12-5204


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


721 F.3d 666; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 34; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 13471


February 19, 2013, Argued 
July 2, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Ass'n of Am. R.R. v. United States DOT, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 20746 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 11, 2013)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Ass'n of Am. R.R. v. United States DOT, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 20745 (D.C. Cir., Oct. 11, 2013)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by DOT v. Ass'n of Am. R.R., 134 S. Ct. 2865, 189 L. Ed. 2d 805, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 4400 (U.S., 2014)
Vacated by, Remanded by DOT v. Ass'n of Am. R.R., 135 S. Ct. 1225, 191 L. Ed. 2d 153, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 1763 (U.S., 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-01499).
Ass'n of Am. R.R. v. DOT, 865 F. Supp. 2d 22, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 74825 (D.D.C., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant railroad trade association sued appellees, including the U.S. Department of Transportation and the Federal Railroad Administration (FRA), in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, challenging the constitutionality of Passenger Rail Investment and Improvement Act of 2008 (PRIIA), Pub. L. No. 110-432, Div. B, § 207, 122 Stat. 4848. The district court granted summary judgment to appellees. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: Section 207 of the PRIIA (49 U.S.C.S. § 24101 note) empowered the National Railroad Passenger Corporation (Amtrak) and the FRA to jointly develop metrics and standards for measuring the performance of passenger train operations. If any intercity passenger train failed to meet those standards, and if the Surface Transportation Board (STB) found that such failure was attributable to a rail carrier's failure to provide preference to Amtrak over freight transportation, the STB could award damages or other relief against the rail carrier. The court of appeals held that § 207 constituted an unlawful delegation of regulatory power to a private entity. The STB's involvement did not did not prevent the statute from being unconstitutional. Further, § 207 permitted Amtrak to petition for binding arbitration if it could not reach agreement with the FRA, which raised the possibility that metrics and standards could have gone into effect without the assent of any government representative. Amtrak, which was required under 49 U.S.C.S. § 24301(a)(2) to operate as a private, for-profit corporation, was a private corporation with respect to Congress's power to delegate regulatory authority.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was reversed.

 

118 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Gordon v. Holder


No. 12-5031 Consolidated with 12-5051


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


721 F.3d 638; 406 U.S. App. D.C. 6; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 13274


October 22, 2012, Argued 
June 28, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-01092).
Gordon v. Holder, 826 F. Supp. 2d 279, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 139201 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee tobacco "delivery seller" sought to enjoin appellant U.S. Attorney General's enforcement of the Prevent All Cigarette Trafficking Act (PACT Act), requiring payment of state and local taxes and banning use of U.S. mail, as violating due process, equal protection, and the Tenth Amendment. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia enjoined enforcement of tax provisions on due process grounds only. Cross-appeals were filed.


OVERVIEW: There was a novel and close question for due process purposes as to whether the seller had to have minimum contacts with the state and local government, which defined and benefited from the tax, or the federal government, which imposed and enforced the tax. Therefore, a finding that the seller was likely to succeed on the merits was not an abuse of discretion. Even a federal duty to comply with state and local tax laws could transgress due process limits on the taxation power. Factual development would benefit the question of whether, in the Internet age, a single sale might establish minimum contacts as a matter of law. PACT Act burdens were different from those that had been held to violate the Tenth Amendment. Congress could pursue permissible policy goals by directly regulating private parties rather than states, which was what Congress did in the PACT Act. The Fifth Amendment due process and equal protection challenges to the ban on shipping tobacco products in the U.S. mail failed under rational basis review. The legislative record showed a rational basis: delivery sellers had been successful at eluding traditional enforcement measures, by making their deliveries by mail.


OUTCOME: The district court's decision was affirmed in its entirety.

Emory v. United Air Lines, Inc.


No. 11-7142, No. 12-5026


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


720 F.3d 915; 405 U.S. App. D.C. 378; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 12724; 118 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1648


October 9, 2012, Argued 
June 21, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Emory v. United Air Lines, Inc., 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 16767 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 12, 2013)
Rehearing denied by Adams v. United States, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 16985 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 14, 2013)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Adams v. United States, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 16978 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 14, 2013)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Emory v. United Air Lines, Inc., 2014 U.S. LEXIS 1870 (U.S., Mar. 10, 2014)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Adams v. United States, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 2013 (U.S., Mar. 24, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-02227).

Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-01646).
Emory v. United Air Lines, Inc., 821 F. Supp. 2d 200, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 121894 (D.D.C., 2011)
Adams v. United States, 796 F. Supp. 2d 67, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 74120 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, pilots over 60 years old, sued defendants, the Federal Aviation Administration and an airline, challenging the constitutionally of 49 U.S.C.S. § 44729(e)(1)'s nonretroactivity provision as to an increased retirement age (Age 65 Rule), the protection-for-compliance provision of § 44729(e)(2), and alleging discriminatory interpretations. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia found for defendants. The pilots appealed.


OVERVIEW: Had Congress given the Fair Treatment for Experienced Pilots Act of 2007 full retroactive effect, airlines might have re-hired a significant number of over-60 pilots with full seniority, thus "bumping" less senior pilots and firing others. Congress thus did not act unreasonably or irrationally in tailoring the retroactive effect to minimize the potential disruption to labor relations in the industry, and an equal protection challenge failed. Just as there had been no procedural due process violation in the prior Age 60 Rule, the nonretroactivity provision to the Age 65 Rule did not violate due process. The Act created more piloting opportunities for over-60 pilots, thus, a bill of attainder claim failed. The "in such operations" language in § 44729(e)(1)(A) modified "that air carrier," not "such person." Because over-60 persons were barred from piloting the covered flights under the Age 60 Rule, only those over-60 persons serving in certain capacities qualified for § 44729(e)(1)(A)'s exemption; that exemption did not apply. The nonretroactivity provision had been properly applied and thus "taken in conformance" with the Age 60 and 65 Rules under § 44729(e)(2).


OUTCOME: The district court was affirmed.

Comcast Cable Communs., LLC v. FCC


No. 12-1337


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


717 F.3d 982; 405 U.S. App. D.C. 188; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 10639; 41 Media L. Rep. 2071; 58 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 539


February 25, 2013, Argued 
May 28, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Comcast Cable Communs. v. FCC, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 18564 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 4, 2013)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Comcast Cable Communs., Inc. v. FCC, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 18565 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 4, 2013)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Tennis Channel, Inc. v. Comcast Cable Communs., LLC, 134 S. Ct. 1287, 188 L. Ed. 2d 301, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 1494 (U.S., 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
Tennis Channel, Inc. v. Comcast Cable Communs., L.L.C., 27 F.C.C.R. 8508, 2012 FCC LEXIS 3182 (F.C.C., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor unaffiliated sport programming network (USPN) sued petitioner multichannel video programming distributor cable company alleging that violations of 47 U.S.C.S. § 536(a)(3) and 47 C.F.R. § 76.1301(c) for refusing to broadcast the USPN as widely as it did its own affiliated sports networks. Respondent Federal Communications Commission affirmed an administrative law judge's order requiring equal carriage to the USPN. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: 47 C.F.R. § 76.1302(h)'s limitations period was not a jurisdictional barrier. There was no adequate evidence of unlawful discrimination. Nothing showed that accepting the USPN's proposal, that its programming be repositioned onto a broader distribution "tier," would have afforded the company any benefit. Nothing refuted the company's contention that rejection was simply a straight up financial analysis, that repositioning would not have resulted in an increase in revenue for the company, let alone revenue sufficient to offset the increased fees. Nothing showed corresponding benefits that would accrue to the company by its accepting the change. The record was very strong on the proposed increment in licensing fees, in itself a clear negative. That the USPN charged less per "rating point" than the affiliated networks was not affirmative evidence that accepting the proposal could have offered the company any net gain. No pretext for some deeper discriminatory purpose was shown. No evidence showed benefits that the company would receive if it distributed the USPN more broadly.


OUTCOME: The petition was granted.

 

121 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Hunter v. FERC


No. 11-1477


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


711 F.3d 155; 404 U.S. App. D.C. 250; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 5167


February 7, 2013, Argued 
March 15, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of Orders of the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission. 2011 FERC LEXIS 789, 2011 FERC LEXIS 2158


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to the Energy Policy Act of 2005, respondent Federal Energy Regulatory Commission (FERC) fined petitioner hedge fund employee $30 million for manipulating natural gas futures contracts. The employee sought review, arguing that FERC lacked authority to fine him because intervenor Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC) had exclusive jurisdiction over all transactions involving commodity futures contracts. CFTC supported the employee.


OVERVIEW: 7 U.S.C.S. § 2(a)(1)(A) gave the CFTC exclusive jurisdiction over transactions conducted on futures markets, and 15 U.S.C.S. § 717t-2(c) of the Natural Gas Act provided that it had no effect on the CFTC's exclusive jurisdiction. FERC fined the employee for trading natural gas futures contracts with the intent to manipulate the price of natural gas in another market. The alleged scheme thus involved transactions of a commodity futures contract. By § 2(a)(1)(A)'s plain terms, the CFTC had exclusive jurisdiction over the manipulation of natural gas futures contracts. Any infringement of the CFTC's exclusive jurisdiction would effectively repeal § 2(a)(1)(A). Because FERC was free to prohibit manipulative trading in markets outside the CFTC's exclusive jurisdiction, there was no irreconcilable conflict between the two statutes and therefore no repeal by implication. By requiring the two agencies to enter into a memorandum of understanding to ensure that information requests to markets within the respective jurisdiction of each agency were properly coordinated, § 717t-2(c)(1) indicated that the two agencies regulates separate markets.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was granted.

Noel Canning v. NLRB

UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

December 5, 2012, Argued 
January 25, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by NLRB v. Canning, 133 S. Ct. 2861, 186 L. Ed. 2d 908, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 4876 (U.S., 2013)
affirmed on other grounds by NLRB v. Noel Canning, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 4500 (U.S., June 26, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review and Cross-Application for Enforcement of an Order of the National Labor Relations Board.
Noel Canning, 2012 NLRB LEXIS 61 (Feb. 8, 2012)



DISPOSITION:  The petition of the company was granted and the NLRB's order was vacated. The cross-petition of the NLRB for enforcement of its invalid order was denied.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner company sought review of a decision of respondent National Labor Relations Board (NLRB) decision finding that the company violated § 8(a)(1) and (5), 29 U.S.C.S. § 158(a)(1), (5), of the National Labor Relations Act by refusing to reduce to writing and execute a collective bargaining agreement reached with a union. The NLRB cross-petitioned for enforcement of its order.


OVERVIEW: On the merits of the NLRB decision, the company argued that the NLRB did not properly follow applicable contract law in determining that an agreement had been reached and that therefore, the finding of unfair labor practice was erroneous. The merits arguments failed. The company also argued that the NLRB lacked authority to act for want of a quorum, as three members of the five-member Board were never validly appointed because they took office under putative recess appointments which were made when the Senate was not in recess. Second, it asserted that the vacancies these three members purportedly filled did not happen during the Recess of the Senate, as required for recess appointments by the U.S. Constitution. The appointments were constitutionally invalid and the Board therefore lacked a quorum. The NLRB conceded that the appointments at issue were not made during the intersession recess: the President made his three appointments to the NLRB on January 4, 2012, after Congress began a new session on January 3 and while that new session continued. Considering the text, history, and structure of the Constitution, these appointments were invalid from their inception.


OUTCOME: The petition of the company was granted and the NLRB's order was vacated. The cross-petition of the NLRB for enforcement of its invalid order was denied.

United States v. Ring

UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT

November 15, 2012, Argued 
January 25, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by United States v. Ring, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 5793 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 21, 2013)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Ring, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 5791 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 21, 2013)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Ring v. United States, 134 S. Ct. 175, 187 L. Ed. 2d 43, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 5467 (U.S., 2013)
Motion denied by United States v. Ring, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 78683 (D.D.C., June 10, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cr-00274-1).
United States v. Ring, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 158237 (D.D.C., Oct. 26, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia of honest-services fraud, paying an illegal gratuity, and conspiracy relating to his provision of meals, tickets, and other gifts to public officials. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the district court's instructions on the honest-services counts misstated the law, the jury lacked sufficient evidence to find that an official act underlay the illegal-gratuity charge, and the district court ran afoul of Fed. R. Evid. 403 and the First Amendment when it admitted evidence of his lawful campaign contributions. The arguments failed. The attorney acted in his official capacity to influence the visa application process. The attorney himself lacked independent authority to expedite visa applications, but defendant's attempt to import a requirement that the official in question was to have ultimate decisionmaking authority into the definition of official act had no statutory basis. The district court repeatedly instructed the jury that the campaign contributions were not illegal which did much to mitigate the potential for confusion and First Amendment chilling, even if it could not entirely eliminate the potential for prejudice. Moreover, the probative value of the contribution evidence and the extent to which it was inexorably intertwined with other evidence weighed heavily in favor of admission.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

Schrader v. Holder


No. 11-5352


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


704 F.3d 980; 403 U.S. App. D.C. 284; 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 730


October 10, 2012, Argued 
January 11, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Schrader v. Holder, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 5039 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 13, 2013)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Schrader v. Holder, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 5044 (D.C. Cir., Mar. 13, 2013)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Schrader V., 2013 U.S. LEXIS 8013 (U.S., Nov. 4, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-01736).
Schrader v. Holder, 831 F. Supp. 2d 304, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 147717 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a misdemeanant and a gun advocacy group, sued defendants, the U.S. Attorney General and others, to challenge 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g)(1) which barred for life common-law misdemeanants from possessing a firearm. Plaintiffs alleged that the application of § 922(g)(1) to that class of offenders violated the Second Amendment. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the complaint. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs argued, in part, that § 922(g)(1) was inapplicable to common-law offenses because such offenses were not punishable by any particular statutory criteria. The court doubted that when Congress expanded the firearms prohibition to cover all individuals convicted of a crime punishable by imprisonment for a term exceeding one year, it intended to exclude all common-law offenses, even those that previously fell within the ambit of the federal firearms ban. The common-sense meaning of the term "punishable" referred to any punishment capable of being imposed, not necessarily a punishment specified by statute. And, because common-law offenses were capable of being punished by more than two years' imprisonment, they were ineligible for § 921(a)(20)(B)'s misdemeanor exception. There was no constitutional infirmity in the application of § 922(g)(1) to common-law misdemeanants as a class. The court applied intermediate scrutiny and concluded that the statute easily met that standard. The disarmament of common-law misdemeanants as a class was substantially related to the important governmental objective of crime prevention.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

Pollack v. Hogan


No. 11-5283


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


703 F.3d 117; 403 U.S. App. D.C. 210; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 25549


November 8, 2012, Argued 
December 14, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment granted by, On remand at Pollack v. Duff, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 110082 (D.D.C., Aug. 6, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00866).
Pollack v. Duff, 806 F. Supp. 2d 99, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 94458 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff job application brought suit against defendant officials of the Administrative Office of the United States Courts in Washington, D.C., solely in their official capacities, alleging that they rejected her job application in violation of her constitutional rights. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the complaint, concluding that it lacked jurisdiction due to the Administrative Office's sovereign immunity.


OVERVIEW: The job applicant, a resident of Kentucky, wanted to work for the Administrative Office, but it rejected her application because she did not live or work in the Washington metropolitan area. Her claim fell within the Larson-Dugan exception. Her sole allegation was that the named officers acted unconstitutionally, and she requested only injunctive and declaratory relief. Although the district court suggested and defendants argued that the Larson-Dugan exception was limited to cases alleging that defendants have acted beyond statutory authority, there was no basis for such a limitation in the logic of the ultra vires rationale for the exception. Moreover, such a limitation contradicted Larson's own language, which excepted suits alleging that the agent acted beyond statutory authority or unconstitutionally. It was also contrary to Dugan, which noted recognized exceptions to the general rule of federal sovereign immunity, for suits alleging that (1) actions by officers were beyond their statutory powers and (2) even though within the scope of their authority, the powers themselves or the manner in which they were exercised are constitutionally void.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

Cellco P'ship v. FCC


No. 11-1135 Consolidated with 11-1136


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


700 F.3d 534; 403 U.S. App. D.C. 105; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 24849; 57 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 135


September 20, 2012, Argued 
December 4, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of and On Appeal from an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
In re Reexamination of Roaming Obligations of Commercial Mobile Radio Serv. Providers, 26 F.C.C.R. 5411, 2011 FCC LEXIS 1364 (F.C.C., 2011)



DISPOSITION: The challenge to the data roaming rule was rejected.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wireless telephone company challenged appellee Federal Communications Commission (FCC) adoption of a rule requiring mobile-data providers to offer roaming agreements to other such providers on commercially reasonable terms. The company argued that the FCC lacked statutory authority to issue the rule and that the rule unlawfully treated mobile-internet providers as common carriers.


OVERVIEW: 47 U.S.C.S. § 303(b) directed the FCC, consistent with the public interest, to prescribe the nature of the service to be rendered by each class of licensed stations and each station within any class. Prescribe meant, among other things, to lay down a rule. That was exactly what the data roaming rule did--it laid down a rule about the nature of the service to be rendered by entities licensed to provide mobile-data service. Especially when taken together with § 303(r), which supplemented the FCC's ability to carry out its mandates via rulemaking even if it confers no independent authority, and 47 U.S.C.S. § 316, which enabled the FCC to alter the term of existing licenses by rulemaking, it was clear that the data roaming rule fell well within the FCC's Title III authority. The FCC's classification of the voice roaming rule as a common carrier obligation did not amount to a conclusion that automatic-roaming requirements necessarily entailed common carriage. The data roaming order did not contravene the statutory exclusion of mobile-internet providers from common carrier status.


OUTCOME: The challenge to the data roaming rule was rejected.

128 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Hamdan v. United States


No. 11-1257


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


696 F.3d 1238; 402 U.S. App. D.C. 471; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 21385


May 3, 2012, Argued 
October 16, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review from the United States Court of Military Commission Review.

United States v. Hamdan, 801 F. Supp. 2d 1247, 2011 U.S. CMCR LEXIS 1, 2011 Military Commission Review 1 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner enemy combatant was accused of being an unlawful enemy combatant and was tried and convicted by a military commission for material support for terrorism in violation of 10 U.S.C.S. § 950t(25), a war crime specified by the Military Commissions Act of 2006. The en banc Court of Military Commission Review affirmed the conviction. The combatant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Despite the combatant's release from custody, his case was not moot. The Military Commissions Act of 2006 did not to authorize retroactive prosecution of crimes that were not prohibited as war crimes triable by military commission under U.S. law at the time the combatant's conduct occurred. When the combatant committed the relevant conduct from 1996 to 2001, 10 U.S.C.S. § 821 provided that military commissions could try violations of the law of war. The "law of war" cross-referenced in that statute was the international law of war. The international law of war proscribed a variety of war crimes, including forms of terrorism. At that time, however, the international law of war did not proscribe material support for terrorism as a war crime. Therefore, the relevant statute at the time of the combatant's conduct -- § 821 -- did not proscribe material support for terrorism as a war crime. Because the circuit court read the Military Commissions Act not to sanction retroactive punishment for new crimes, and because material support for terrorism was not a pre-existing war crime under § 821, the combatant's conviction for material support for terrorism could not stand.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and it was directed that the combatant's conviction for material support for terrorism be vacated.

136 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Miller v. Clinton


No. 10-5405


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


687 F.3d 1332; 402 U.S. App. D.C. 106; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 16343; 115 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1157; 96 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P44,608


October 14, 2011, Argued 
August 7, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment granted by, Motion granted by, Request denied by, Motion denied by, Without prejudice Miller v. Kerry, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 22524 (D.D.C., Feb. 20, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00512).
Miller v. Clinton, 750 F. Supp. 2d 11, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 117434 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After being dismissed on his sixty-fifth birthday, plaintiff employee brought suit alleging that his forced retirement violated the federal employment provisions of the Age Discrimination in Employment Act (ADEA), 29 U.S.C.S. § 633a. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a U.S. citizen who was employed by the State Department as a safety inspector at the U.S. embassy in Paris, France. He was hired as "locally employed staff" pursuant to a personal services agreement One provision of the Local Compensation Plan was a mandatory retirement clause that followed the (apparently) prevailing French practice of mandating retirement at age sixty-five. The district court dismissed the complaint on the ground that the statute under which the employee was hired, § 2(c) of the Basic Authorities Act, 22 U.S.C.S. § 2669(c), permitted the Department to exempt an employee from ADEA protections. Inter alia, the appellate court held that the second clause of 22 U.S.C.S. § 2669(c) did not give the Department the authority to fire the employee solely because he turned sixty-five. Neither that clause; nor § 408 of the Foreign Service Act, to which the Department claimed the clause referred, nor the further sections of the Foreign Service Act that § 408 itself referenced, spoke with the kind of clarity one would expect if Congress had intended to remove the protections of landmark legislation like the ADEA from a class of U.S. citizens.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court, granting the State Department's motion to dismiss the ADEA claim was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

138 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Burwell


No. 06-3070


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


690 F.3d 500; 402 U.S. App. D.C. 193; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 16094


January 30, 2012, Argued 
August 3, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Burwell v. United States, 133 S. Ct. 1459, 185 L. Ed. 2d 368, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 1832 (U.S., 2013)
Post-conviction relief denied at United States v. Stoddard, 74 F. Supp. 3d 332, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 164476 (D.D.C., 2014)
Post-conviction relief denied at, Motion denied by, Certificate of appealability denied United States v. Morrow, 102 F. Supp. 3d 232, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 56647 (D.D.C., 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 04cr00355-05).
United States v. Burwell, 642 F.3d 1062, 395 U.S. App. D.C. 241, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 8683 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Columbia sentenced appellant to concurrent prison terms of 135 months for RICO conspiracy and armed bank robbery, 60 months for conspiracy to commit armed bank robbery, and a consecutive term of 360 months for using or carrying a machinegun during the robbery. After an appeal before the instant court, appellant sought rehearing en banc.


OVERVIEW: Only a single legal question remained: whether 18 U.S.C.S. § 924(c)(1)(B)(ii), which imposed a mandatory 30 year sentence for any person who carried a machinegun while committing a crime of violence, required the government to prove that a defendant knew the weapon he was carrying was capable of firing automatically. Appellant argued the government presented insufficient evidence to support his conviction under § 924(c)(1)(B)(ii) because the government failed to show he knew the AK-47 he carried was capable of firing automatically. By claiming § 924(c)(1)(B)(ii) contained an additional, implicit mens rea requirement, appellant asked the instant court to set aside a circuit precedent that governed its interpretation for 20 years. Appellant and his amici failed to establish that any intervening legal development weakened, much less removed, the conceptual underpinnings of the instant court's decision in Harris. It could not be said that Congress simply forgot about mens rea when it drafted § 924(c); § 924(c)(1)(B)(ii) did not require the government to prove that a defendant knew that the weapon he used, carried, or possessed was a machinegun.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction was affirmed.

 

139 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Philip Morris USA Inc.


No. 11-5145


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


686 F.3d 832; 402 U.S. App. D.C. 34; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 15529


April 20, 2012, Argued 
July 27, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:99-cv-02496).
United States v. Philip Morris USA, Inc., 787 F. Supp. 2d 68, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 58205 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee United States sued appellants, cigarette manufacturers and organizations, for civil violations of the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO). The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia imposed injunctions. The court affirmed all but four aspects of the order and remanded for further proceedings. Appellants moved to vacate the injunctions. The district court denied the motion. Appellants filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellants argued that the Family Smoking Prevention and Tobacco Control Act, Pub. L. No. 111-31, 123 Stat. 1776 (2009), deprived the district court of jurisdiction by eliminating any reasonable likelihood they would have committed future RICO violations. The court found that the Tobacco Control Act did not make it impossible to grant effectual relief because it did not make it impossible for appellants to commit future RICO violations. If appellants were not deterred by the possibility of RICO liability, appellants were not likely to be deterred by the Tobacco Control Act either. The district court maintained jurisdiction because it applied the correct legal standard, and did not clearly err in finding appellants still exhibited a reasonable likelihood of committing future RICO violations. The district court did not abuse its discretion when it refused to vacate its injunctions under the primary jurisdiction doctrine because, while the Tobacco Control Act gave the Food and Drug Administration (FDA) the authority to regulate much of appellants' conduct, it gave the FDA no particular insight into whether appellants were likely to comply with those restrictions.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Spirit Airlines, Inc. v. United States DOT


No. 11-1219 Consolidated with 11-1222


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


687 F.3d 403; 402 U.S. App. D.C. 70; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 15189


May 7, 2012, Argued 
July 24, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Spirit Airlines v. Dep't of Transp., 2013 U.S. LEXIS 2713 (U.S., Apr. 1, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of Final Rules of the U.S. Department of Transportation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner airlines sought review of final rules of the U.S. Department of Transportation final rule entitled Enhancing Airline Passenger Protections. 76 Fed. Reg. 23,110 (Apr. 25, 2011) as arbitrary and capricious, a violation of the First Amendment, and as procedurally defective.


OVERVIEW: The airlines challenged a requirement that the most prominent figure displayed on print ads and websites be the total price, including taxes (Advertising Rule); a requirement that consumers who purchased tickets more than a week in advance be allowed to cancel reservations without penalty for twenty-four hours after purchase (Refund Rule); and a prohibition against increasing ticket and baggage fees after purchase (Price Rule). Inter alia, the court held that nothing in the Advertising Rule required airlines to hide the taxes. It just required that the total, final price be the most prominently listed figure. This was not an arbitrary exercise of DOT's authority to prevent unfair or deceptive practices, 49 U.S.C.S. § 41712(a). The First Amendment plainly permitted a rule that simply regulated the manner of disclosure and imposed no burden on speech other than requiring disclosure of total price. The Refund Rule--developed as part of a systematic effort aimed at preventing unfair and deceptive practices--regulated, not airfares, but only cancellations, and was not arbitrary or capricious. The DOT was undertaking a new notice and comment procedure before enforcing the Price Rule.


OUTCOME: The petitions for review were denied.

Rural Cellular Ass'n & Universal Serv. v. FCC


No. 11-1094


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


685 F.3d 1083; 401 U.S. App. D.C. 459; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 14349; 56 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 458


November 15, 2011, Argued 
July 13, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
In re High-Cost Universal Serv. Support et al., 25 F.C.C.R. 18146, 2010 FCC LEXIS 7534 (F.C.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner trade groups sought review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission (FCC) amending the interim cap rule, which limited at 2008 levels the amount of support available to competitive eligible telecommunications carriers (CETCs) through the High-Cost Universal Service Support Program.


OVERVIEW: The FCC amended the interim cap rule to provide that when a carrier relinquished its eligible communications carrier status, the cap on support available in the carrier's state was reduced by the amount the relinquishing carrier would have received had it retained its status. Inter alia, the appellate court held that the order was a lawful exercise of FCC authority because the Universal Service Administration Company was no longer required to dispose of excess funds by lowering the next quarter's contribution factor and the waiver of 47 C.F.R. § 54.709(b) did staunch the outflow of funds. What kept the inflow of funds above actual expenses each quarter was the directive to continue collecting contributions "as if" the cap had not been lowered. Each action was necessary to create the "temporary reserve" by setting the level of Program contributions higher than the level of Program disbursements. As interpreted by the FCC, the Act neither "raised revenue" under the Origination Clause nor delegated Congress's authority to "lay and collect taxes" in contravention of the Taxing Clause. So the constitutional avoidance canon gave no reason to reject the FCC's interpretation.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was denied.

Intercollegiate Broad. Sys. v. Copyright Royalty Bd.


No. 11-1083


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


684 F.3d 1332; 401 U.S. App. D.C. 407; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 13757; Copy. L. Rep. (CCH) P30,276; 103 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1337


February 7, 2012, Argued 
July 6, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Intercollegiate Broad. Sys., Inc v. Copyright Royalty Bd. & Library of Cong., 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 18229 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 28, 2012)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Intercollegiate Broad. Sys. v. Copyright Royalty Bd., 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 18230 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 28, 2012)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Intercollegiate Broad. Sys. v. Copyright Royalty Bd., 133 S. Ct. 2735, 186 L. Ed. 2d 192, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 4003 (U.S., 2013)
Decision reached on appeal by Intercollegiate Broad. Sys. v. Copyright Royalty Bd. & Librarian of Cong., 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 14015 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 11, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Appeal from a Final Order of the Copyright Royalty Board.
Intercollegiate Broad. Sys. v. Copyright Royalty Bd., 574 F.3d 748, 387 U.S. App. D.C. 387, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 17674 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Copyright Royalty Judges (CRJs) issued a final determination adopting as statutory rates a royalty structure agreed to in a settlement between intervenor, a non-profit musicians' webcast royalty payment clearinghouse, and appellant, a nonprofit web-based broadcasters' association, and declining to establish different fee structures for "small" and "very small" noncommercial webcasters. the association sought review under 17 U.S.C.S. § 803(d)(1).


OVERVIEW: The challenged rates concerned transmission of digitally recorded music over the internet in educational environments , which were "performances" under the Copyright Act and thus entitled the owner of a song's copyright to royalty payments. 17 U.S.C.S. § 106(6). The parties sought review the decision for compliance with 17 U.S.C.S. § 114(f)(2)(A). Inter alia, the court held that the CRJs, as currently established, were principal officers who had to be appointed by the President and confirmed by the Senate, and that the structure of the Copyright Royalty Board therefore violated the Appointments Clause. U.S. Const., art. II, § 2, cl. 2. To remedy the violation, the court invalidated and severed the restrictions on the Librarian of Congress's ability to remove the CRJs. With such removal power in the Librarian's hands, the Judges were inferior rather than principal officers, and no constitutional problem remained. Because of the Appointments Clause violation at the time of decision, the court vacated and remanded the determination challenged here; accordingly it did not need to reach appellant's arguments regarding the merits of the rates and terms set in that determination.


OUTCOME: Because the Board's structure was unconstitutional at the time it issued its determination, the court vacated and remanded the determination and did not address arguments regarding the merits of the rates set therein.

Ass'n of Private Sector Colleges & Univs. v. Duncan


No. 11-5174 Consolidated with 11-5230


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


681 F.3d 427; 401 U.S. App. D.C. 96; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 11269


February 21, 2012, Argued 
June 5, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:11-cv-00138).
Career College Ass'n v. Duncan, 796 F. Supp. 2d 108, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 74616 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, an association of private colleges and universities sued defendant, the Department and Secretary of Education, challenging the State Authorization, Compensation, and Misrepresentation Regulations under 5 U.S.C.S. § 706 (2006) and the Constitution. Both parties moved for summary judgment. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted the Department summary judgment in almost all respects. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: Inter alia, the appellate court affirmed the judgment of the District Court, holding that the Compensation Regulations did not exceed Title IV of the Higher Education Act of 1965 (HEA), Pub. L. No. 89-329, 79 Stat. 1219, 123-254, limits, and mostly rejected the claim that the regulations were not based on reasoned decision making. The court remanded two aspects of the Compensation Regulations, however, that lacked adequate explanations. The court also held that the Misrepresentation Regulations exceeded the HEA's limits by allowing the Secretary of Education to take enforcement actions against schools sans procedural protections, proscribing misrepresentations as to subjects that were not covered by the HEA, and by proscribing statements that were merely confusing. The court rejected the remaining challenges to the Misrepresentation Regulations. With respect to the State Authorization Regulations, plaintiff had standing to challenge the school authorization regulation, but the court held that the regulation was valid. However, the court upheld the challenge to the distance education regulation, because that regulation was not a logical outgrowth of the Department's proposed rules.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part and the case was remanded to the district court with instructions to remand the challenged regulations to the Department of Education for reconsideration consistent with this opinion.
