Nat'l Ass'n of Regulatory Util. Comm'rs v. United States DOE


No. 11-1066 Consolidated with 11-1068


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


680 F.3d 819; 401 U.S. App. D.C. 15; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 11044; 42 ELR 20120; 75 ERC (BNA) 1010


April 20, 2012, Argued 
June 1, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Nat'l Ass'n of Regulatory Util. Comm'rs v. United States DOE, 2013 U.S. App. LEXIS 23246 (D.C. Cir., Nov. 19, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: On Petitions for Review of Final Actions of the Department of Energy.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, nuclear power plant owners and operators, sought review of a determination by respondent Secretary of Energy which found that there was no basis for suspending, or otherwise adjusting, annual fees collected from them totaling some $750 million a year. The fees were intended to cover the full costs of the government's long-term disposal of civilian nuclear waste.


OVERVIEW: According to petitioners, the Secretary's determination, made subsequent to the decision to discontinue development of the only designated location for the disposal of the waste, failed to examine the anticipated costs of disposal, or compare them to expected revenues from the fees in violation of the Nuclear Waste Policy Act of 1982, which obliged the Secretary to annually evaluate whether collection of the fee would provide sufficient revenues to offset program costs. The court agreed that the Secretary failed to perform a valid evaluation, but the court did not think it appropriate to order the suspension of the fee at that time and instead concluded that a remand was appropriate. Although the Secretary had some discretion in the manner in which he calculated costs, the government's argument suggested the Secretary had no affirmative obligation to conduct the sort of inquiry and analysis done in the past. The Secretary could not rely on the 2008 assessment's cost calculations for the former designated location as a proxy. Moreover, the 2010 determination was an unexplained departure from long-standing Department policy and arbitrary and capricious on that ground as well.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter to the Secretary to make a proper assessment within six months.

 

145 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Billington


No. 11-5092


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


681 F.3d 377; 401 U.S. App. D.C. 46; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 11042; 33 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1684


November 10, 2011, Argued 
June 1, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00036).
Davis v. Billington, 775 F. Supp. 2d 23, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 33594 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee, who worked for a division of the Library of Congress, sued, inter alia, defendant former supervisor, alleging that his termination for publication of articles critical of high-level public officials violated the First and Fifth Amendments and entitled him to damages under Bivens. Defendant moved to dismiss. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia denied the motion. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff criticized the system of military commissions created to prosecute suspected terrorists held at Guantanamo Bay Naval Base, Cuba, and officials who chose to prosecute some detainees in federal courts and others in military commissions. The court had previously held against applying the Bivens model to claims of First Amendment violations by federal employees. The court would not imply a Bivens remedy where Congress had adopted a comprehensive remedial scheme, and in the instant case, the scheme was the Civil Service Reform Act (CSRA). The CSRA's remedial scheme provided procedural protections and rights of appeal for the specific underlying actions that plaintiff challenged. The CSRA's lack of relief for plaintiff (due to his status as a probationary employee of a non-Executive agency) did not prevent the CSRA from being a comprehensive remedial scheme that precluded the court from creating a Bivens remedy. The CSRA's remedial scheme evinced a considered judgment of Congress that certain remedies were not warranted. The court gave appropriate judicial deference to that judgment and thus declined to create a Bivens remedy.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the district court denying defendant's motion to dismiss and remanded with instructions to dismiss plaintiff's Bivens claims.

Shelby County v. Holder


No. 11-5256


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


679 F.3d 848; 400 U.S. App. D.C. 367; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 10027


January 19, 2012, Argued 
May 18, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, in part Shelby County v. Holder, 133 S. Ct. 594, 184 L. Ed. 2d 389, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 8699 (U.S., 2012)
Reversed by Shelby County v. Holder, 133 S. Ct. 2612, 186 L. Ed. 2d 651, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 4917 (U.S., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00651).
Shelby County v. Holder, 811 F. Supp. 2d 424, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 107305 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county, a covered jurisdiction under the Voting Rights Act of 1965 (VRA), sued defendant U.S. Attorney General, seeking both a declaratory judgment that VRA §§ 4(b) and 5 were facially unconstitutional and a permanent injunction prohibiting the Attorney General from enforcing them. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted summary judgment for the Attorney General. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contended that when Congress reauthorized § 5 of the VRA, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973c, in 2006, it exceeded its enumerated powers. In Northwest Austin, the U.S. Supreme Court signaled that the extraordinary federalism costs imposed by § 5 raised substantial constitutional concerns. As a lower federal court urged to strike this duly enacted law of Congress, the appellate court proceeded with great caution, bound as it was by Supreme Court precedent and confined as it had to be to resolve only the precise legal question before it: Did the severe remedy of preclearance remain congruent and proportional? The legislative record was by no means unambiguous. But Congress drew reasonable conclusions from the extensive evidence it gathered and acted pursuant to the Fourteenth and Fifteenth Amendments, which entrusted Congress with ensuring that the right to vote--surely among the most important guarantees of political liberty in the Constitution--was not abridged on account of race. In this context, the court owed much deference to the considered judgment of the people's elected representatives, so it declined to find the legislation unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed.

Timbisha Shoshone Tribe v. Salazar


No. 11-5049


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


678 F.3d 935; 400 U.S. App. D.C. 336; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 9740


November 17, 2011, Argued

Timbisha Shoshone Tribe v. Salazar, 766 F. Supp. 2d 175, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 19745 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, individuals claiming to be the tribal council of an Indian tribe, filed suit, arguing that the Western Shoshone Claims Distribution Act, Pub. L. No. 108-270, § 3, 118 Stat. 805, 806, was an unconstitutional taking of tribal property. Plaintiffs appealed from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of the case for failure to state a claim.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs conceded they lacked standing to bring suit as individuals, but alleged they were the tribal council acting in its official capacity to protect the interests of the tribe. However, there had been a leadership dispute in the tribe, and shortly after the district court's decision, the tribe held a special election, and the other faction prevailed over plaintiffs. The Assistant Secretary for Indian Affairs recognized the tribal council led by the other faction in a letter. The tribe resolved their own leadership dispute through a valid internal tribal process. Although plaintiffs were unhappy with how the election was run, who voted, and the results, this was not the forum for that debate.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the district court and remanded with instructions to dismiss the complaint for lack of jurisdiction.

 

148 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Van Hollen v. FEC


No. 12-5117 Consolidated with 12-5118


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 10333



May 14, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
1:11-cv-00766-ABJ.
Van Hollen v. FEC, 851 F. Supp. 2d 69, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 44342 (D.D.C., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a member of Congress, brought a challenge to 11 C.F.R. § 104.20(c)(9), a regulation promulgated by defendant Federal Election Commission. The district court vacated the regulation. Intervenors appealed. Intervenors filed a motion for an emergency stay of the district court's order or, alternatively, to expedite the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Intervenors challenged the district court's interpretation of § 201 (2 U.S.C.S. § 434(f)(2)(E) & (F)) of the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act (BCRA). The court of appeals found that intervenors failed to make a strong showing that they were likely to succeed on the merits, nor did they demonstrate that they would be irreparably injured absent a stay. The plain text of § 434(f)(2)(F) required disclosures to contain the names of all contributors who contributed an aggregate amount of $1,000 or more to a person purchasing "electioneering communications." The U.S. Supreme Court's decision to strike a separate provision of the BCRA did not alter the interpretation in light of the BCRA's severability provision, 2 U.S.C.S. § 454. The definition of "contribution" under the Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971 was inapplicable. Intervenors provided no evidence that their contributors would face threats, harassment, or reprisals if their names were disclosed and therefore failed to demonstrate how the disclosure requirements would prevent them from speaking.


OUTCOME: The emergency motion for a stay pending appeal was denied. The appeal was expedited.

150 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Youngbey v. March


No. 11-7033


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


676 F.3d 1114; 400 U.S. App. D.C. 177; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 7630


January 12, 2012, Argued 
April 17, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-00596).
Youngbey v. District of Columbia, 766 F. Supp. 2d 197, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 19748 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs brought an action arising under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 against defendant law enforcement officers, claiming, inter alia, violations of their Fourth Amendment rights. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia rejected the officers' claims of qualified immunity. The officers sought interlocutory review.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs claimed that the officers violated their Fourth Amendment rights by planning and conducting a 4:00 a.m. search on a warrant that did not authorize a nighttime search and breaking and entering into their home without knocking and announcing their presence. The district court rejected the officers' qualified immunity claims, finding that the failure to knock and announce before entering into plaintiffs' home and the nighttime search violated their clearly established rights under the Fourth Amendment. The question was whether plaintiffs' asserted rights were clearly established when the officers executed the search warrant. The appellate court held that, since the court found no controlling precedent or consensus of persuasive authority from other circuits, there was no clearly established law. As neither the no-knock entry of plaintiffs' home nor the nighttime search violated clearly established law, the officers were entitled to qualified immunity.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court on these issues was reversed, and the case was remanded for trial on the remaining issues.

United States ex rel. Schweizer v. Océ N.V.


No. 11-7030


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


677 F.3d 1228; 400 U.S. App. D.C. 284; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 8000


January 13, 2012, Argued 
April 10, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Reissued April 20, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:06-cv-00648).
United States ex rel. Schweizer v. Oce N. Am., Inc., 772 F. Supp. 2d 174, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 31204 (D.D.C., 2011)
United States ex rel. Schweizer v. Oce N.V., 681 F. Supp. 2d 64, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 9890 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator sued defendant, her former employer, under the False Claims Act's qui tam and retaliation provisions, 31 U.S.C.S. § 3730(b) & (h). The government moved to dismiss the qui tam claims after reaching a settlement agreement with defendant. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted the motion over relator's objection and granted defendant summary judgment on the remaining retaliation claim. Relator appealed.


OVERVIEW: Relator, hired to monitor defendant's governmental contract compliance, suspected defendant was violating price reduction clauses and regularly overcharging government agencies. She continued to investigate despite being told by a supervisor to stop doing so. Her employment eventually was terminated. The government declined to intervene in the qui tam action at first but pursued a settlement, pursuant to which it eventually intervened and moved to dismiss. Relator opposed the settlement and dismissal. Inter alia, the appellate court held that the settlement agreement fell within § 3730(c)(2)(B), which gave the judiciary the final word on settlement decisions. The district court erred in dismissing without finding the settlement agreement fair, adequate, and reasonable. Relator's factual allegations were sufficient to overcome the presumption that she was merely acting in accordance with her employment obligations. She set forth a prima facie retaliation case and defendant presented an alternative, non-discriminatory basis for terminating her employment. So all that remained was the ultimate question of whether a reasonable jury could infer retaliation from all the evidence.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed on all counts and remanded for review of the settlement agreement pursuant to 31 U.S.C.S. § 3730(c)(2)(B) and consideration of the parties' remaining summary judgment arguments on the § 3730(h) retaliation claim.

Talal Al-Zahrani v. Rodriguez


No. 10-5393


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


669 F.3d 315; 399 U.S. App. D.C. 258; 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 3320


October 6, 2011, Argued 
February 21, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-00028).
Al-Zahrani v. Rumsfeld, 684 F. Supp. 2d 103, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 13282 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff representatives of the estates of their deceased sons, brought an action against defendant federal officials seeking money damages relating to the alleged mistreatment and eventual death of those sons while they were detained at Guantanamo Bay Naval Base, Cuba. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia granted the motion of the United States to be substituted as defendant and the motion of the United States for dismissal.


OVERVIEW: Beginning in January of 2002, the first decedent, a citizen of Saudi Arabia, and the second decedent, a citizen of Yemen, were detained at the United States military base at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, as enemy combatants. On June 10 of 2006, both men, along with a third detainee, died. Although the cause of death was the subject of dispute, a Naval Criminal Investigative Service report concluded that the deaths were the result of suicide by hanging. The District Court, while recognizing that the plain language of 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e)(2) precluded jurisdiction over claims by aliens who have been determined to be enemy combatants by the United States, decided to avoid the constitutional implications it saw in the jurisdictional question and instead reached the merits of the complaint and entered a dismissal under Fed. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6). The litigation plainly constituted an action other than habeas corpus brought against the United States and its agents relating to aspects of the detention treatment and conditions of confinement of an alien as described in 28 U.S.C.S. § 2241(e)(1) and (2). Therefore, the action was excluded from the jurisdiction of the court by an Act of Congress.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed although for lack of jurisdiction rather than failure to state a claim.

N.J. v. EPA


No. 05-1097 Consolidated with 05-1104, 05-1116, 05-1118, 05-1158, 05-1159, 05-1160, 05-1162, 05-1163, 05-1164, 05-1167, 05-1174, 05-1175, 05-1176, 05-1183, 05-1189, 05-1263, 05-1267, 05-1270, 05-1271, 05-1275, 05-1277, 06-1211, 06-1220, 06-1231, 06-1287, 06-1291, 06-1293, 06-1294


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


663 F.3d 1279; 398 U.S. App. D.C. 343; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 25810; 74 ERC (BNA) 1059



December 20, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at, Motion granted by New Jersey v. EPA, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 26118 (D.C. Cir., Dec. 20, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: New Jersey v. EPA, 517 F.3d 574, 380 U.S. App. D.C. 134, 2008 U.S. App. LEXIS 2797 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenors, a group of Native American tribes and associations, intervened on behalf of petitioners, a state and others, who were challenging Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) rules regulating mercury emissions from power plants. Respondent EPA agreed to petitioners' fees once they prevailed. Intervenors then moved for their fees and costs under 42 U.S.C.S. § 7607(f) of the Clean Air Act. The EPA objected, claiming that they were ineligible.


OVERVIEW: Intervenors entered the litigation on the side of petitioners, not the government, and they offered two substantial arguments based on the EPA's alleged failure to consider intervenors' treaty rights, arguments that petitioners lacked standing to make. Although the court never reached intervenors' arguments, that was immaterial. By giving the court alternative bases for resolving the case--bases petitioners were unable to offer--the intervenors contributed to the proper implementation and administration of the Act or otherwise served the public interest. The EPA's view, embraced by the dissent--that the court should allow fee shifting only where an intervenor affected the outcome of a case as determined after the fact--would have discouraged interventions that played a useful role. Intervenors wasted no time duplicating petitioners' arguments. Instead, they focused on arguments that petitioners did not and could not make. In the court's view, their contribution was precisely the type that § 7607(f) sought to incentivize.


OUTCOME: The court granted intervenors' request for fees and costs. The court directed the parties to direct the question of the amount of fees to its appellate mediation program.

Altman v. SEC


No. 11-1067


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


666 F.3d 1322; 399 U.S. App. D.C. 55; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 24954


November 10, 2011, Argued 
December 16, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Securities & Exchange Commission.
Altman v. United States SEC, 768 F. Supp. 2d 554, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 23230 (S.D.N.Y., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review of an opinion and order of the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) permanently denying him the privilege of appearing or practicing before the SEC, pursuant to Rule 102(e)(1)(ii) of the SEC's Rules of Practice, and § 4C of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934 (Act), 15 U.S.C.S. § 78d-3(a)(2). The attorney also petitioned for review of the SEC's denial of his motion for reconsideration and a stay.


OVERVIEW: The SEC found that the attorney, in appearing before it, violated three Disciplinary Rules of the New York Bar Association Lawyer's Code of Professional Responsibility, and that the violations were egregious, recurrent, and reflected a high degree of scienter. The court held that § 4C of the Act authorized the SEC to deny the privilege of appearance upon finding improper professional conduct. The SEC was entitled to rely on the attorney's knowledge of and duty to conform to the New York Bar disciplinary rules. Also, the attorney was on notice that, based on the New York Bar disciplinary rules, the SEC could proceed against him under Rule 102(e)(1)(ii) and § 4C of the Act to protect the integrity of its processes. The court held that the SEC's factual findings were supported by substantial evidence in the record and its choice of sanction was statutorily authorized under § 4C of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for review.

Ibrahim v. United States


No. 09-5052


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


661 F.3d 1141; 398 U.S. App. D.C. 227; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 23735


October 7, 2011, Argued 
November 29, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-02130).
Ibrahim v. United States, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 99916 (D.D.C., Nov. 6, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: As a result of multiple convictions, petitioner inmate was serving an aggregate life sentence imposed by District of Columbia courts. He filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia which dismissed the petition for want of jurisdiction, reasoning that the inmate was required to pursue his challenge in D.C. Superior Court rather than federal court. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: Although the exact nature of the inmate's constitutional claims was not clear, they involved actual innocence coupled with a constitutional violation and miscarriage of justice and other due process and equal protection of law claims. The reason the inmate provided for not being able to bring these constitutional claims under D.C. Code § 23-110(a) was that his actual innocence allegations were tethered to alleged constitutional violations and accordingly, in light of caselaw, the Innocence Protection Act, D.C. Code § 22-4131 et seq., provided the exclusive judicial remedy for District of Columbia offenders who obtain new evidence of actual innocence more than three years after they are convicted. However, this was a misreading of caselaw. The circuit court concluded that jurists of reason would not find the inmate's claim that the district court had jurisdiction debatable. In essence this was because caselaw could not reasonably be read to bar the inmate from bringing his federal constitutional claims in D.C. Superior Court under D.C. Code § 23-110. Accordingly the certificate of appealability was denied.


OUTCOME: The request for a certificate of appealability was denied.

Seven-Sky v. Holder


No. 11-5047


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


661 F.3d 1; 398 U.S. App. D.C. 134; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 22566; 2011-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50,713; 108 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 7074; 53 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1993


September 23, 2011, Argued 
November 8, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Seven-Sky v. Holder, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 4945 (U.S., June 29, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00950).
Mead v. Holder, 766 F. Supp. 2d 16, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 18592 (D.D.C., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, citizens and taxpayers, appealed an order from the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia rejecting their constitutional challenge to the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act and denying their claims for declaratory and injunctive relief. Appellants argued in particular that the minimum essential coverage provision found at 26 U.S.C.S. § 5000A exceeded Congress's authority under the Commerce Clause.


OVERVIEW: As a threshold matter, the court rejected any challenge to its subject matter jurisdiction, finding that the Anti-Injunction Act did not, by its terms, cover the shared responsibility payment provision for not complying with the individual mandate under the term "any tax." Congress did not intend for "any tax" to include exactions unrelated to taxes that Congress labeled "penalties." The court read the shared responsibility payment under 26 U.S.C.S. § 5000A(g)(1) as not implicating the Anti-Injunction Act. As for the Commerce Clause challenge, it was irrelevant that an indeterminate number of healthy, uninsured persons would never consume health care and would therefore never affect the interstate market. Congress reasonably determined that as a class, the uninsured created market failures; thus, the lack of harm attributable to any particular uninsured individual, like their lack of overt participation in a market, was of no consequence. The right to be free from federal regulation was not absolute, and yielded to the imperative that Congress be free to forge national solutions to national problems, no matter how local--or seemingly passive--their individual origins.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the district court.

Heller v. District of Columbia


No. 10-7036


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


670 F.3d 1244; 399 U.S. App. D.C. 314; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 20130


November 15, 2010, Argued 
October 4, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Heller v. D.C., 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 38833 (D.D.C., Mar. 20, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-01289).
Heller v. District of Columbia, 698 F. Supp. 2d 179, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 29063 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs challenged, both facially and as applied to them, the provisions of the District of Columbia's gun laws, new and old, requiring the registration of firearms and prohibiting both the registration of assault weapons and the possession of magazines with a capacity of more than ten rounds of ammunition. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted summary judgment for the District and the plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The plaintiffs argued the provisions (1) were not within the District's congressionally delegated legislative authority or, if they were, then they (2) violated the Second Amendment. The appellate court held that the District had the authority under D.C. law to promulgate the challenged gun laws. Using the Heller framework, the court upheld the requirement of mere registration because it was longstanding and hence, presumptively lawful, and the presumption stood unrebutted. The court also applied intermediate scrutiny to uphold as constitutional the prohibitions of assault weapons and of large-capacity magazines. However, the court remanded the other registration requirements to the district court for further proceedings because the record was insufficient to inform its resolution of the important constitutional issues presented.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the district court with respect to the handgun registration requirement and the ban on assault weapons and large-capacity magazines. With respect to the registration requirements as applied to long guns, the judgment was vacated and this matter remanded to the district court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

Anderson v. Holder


No. 10-5097


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


647 F.3d 1165; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 281; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 16856


April 18, 2011, Argued 
August 16, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-01197).
Anderson v. Holder, 691 F. Supp. 2d 57, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 19983 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sued defendants, the United States and the District of Columbia, in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, challenging the constitutionality of the District of Columbia Sex Offender Registration Act (SORA), D.C. Code §§ 22-4001 to 22-4017. The district court granted defendants' motion to dismiss. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: Prior to enactment of SORA, plaintiff was convicted of assault with intent to commit rape. He argued that SORA's sex offender registration requirement as applied to him violated the Ex Post Facto Clause. The court of appeals held that there was no ex post facto violation because SORA was civil and nonpunitive in nature. SORA was intended by the District of Columbia Council to be nonpunitive; significantly, the work of SORA was assigned to an administrative agency. SORA also was not punitive in purpose or effect. Sex offender registration schemes had not been traditionally regarded as a punishment, and allowing the police to notify the community of plaintiff's status did not make SORA excessive with respect to its civil and nonpunitive purpose. Plaintiff also alleged that his Fifth Amendment rights were violated because he was forced to participate in polygraph examinations and psychotherapy sessions, but he did not allege that he was exposed to the threat of incrimination, was compelled to testify, or asserted his privilege against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed.

Affirmed by Smith v. United States, 133 S. Ct. 714, 184 L. Ed. 2d 570, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 601 (U.S., 2013)
Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by United States v. McGill, 815 F.3d 846, 2016 U.S. App. LEXIS 3734 (D.C. Cir., 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:00-cr-00157).
United States v. Gray, 292 F. Supp. 2d 71, 2003 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 21063 (D.D.C., 2003)
United States v. Gray, 254 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2002 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26035 (D.D.C., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Six defendants were charged in a 158-count superseding indictment upon which the defendants were tried by a United States District Court for the District of Columbia jury and convicted on multiple charges, including drug conspiracy, Racketeering Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act conspiracy, continuing criminal enterprise, murder, and other related charges in violation of federal and District of Columbia laws. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants and other separately tried co-conspirators conspired and conducted an ongoing drug distribution business. In the course of conducting that business, various co-conspirators committed a wide-ranging course of violence, including 31 murders. They asserted a variety of alleged errors including evidentiary issues, both as to admission and sufficiency; conduct of the trial; prosecutorial misconduct; and jury instructions. Upon review, the appellate court concluded that most of the asserted errors either were not erroneous or were harmless. As to one category of issue involving alleged Confrontation Clause violations, an intervening Supreme Court decision compelled remand of the convictions of some drug charges. On one defendant's claim that his counsel erroneously believed there was a Bruton problem and therefore failed to call an important witness, the record was inconclusive as to whether the allegations reflected counsel's informed tactical choice or a decision undertaken out of ignorance of the relevant law. Thus, the claim was remanded to the district court for an evidentiary hearing. The appellate court also vacated one murder conviction that merged with another.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed all of defendant's convictions except a murder conviction that merged with another count, which it vacated, two counts which the court remanded for an evidentiary hearing and to address the ineffective assistance of counsel claim; and several counts involving alleged Confrontation Clause violations, which it remanded for consideration in light of intervening U.S. Supreme Court caselaw.

Lake Carriers' Ass'n v. EPA


No. 09-1001 Consolidated with 09-1010, 09-1076, 09-1115


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


652 F.3d 1; 397 U.S. App. D.C. 269; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 14996; 41 ELR 20250; 73 ERC (BNA) 1870


May 9, 2011, Argued 
July 22, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of a Final Action of the Environmental Protection Agency.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner trade associations representing commercial ship owners and operators petitioned for review of the final action establishing a Vessel General Permit pursuant to § 402 (33 U.S.C.S. § 1342) of the Clean Water Act (CWA), 33 U.S.C.S. § 1251 et seq., by respondent Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) for the discharge of pollutants incidental to the normal operation of vessels.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners' contended that the EPA failed to provide notice and an opportunity for comment before promulgating the final permit, as required by 5 U.S.C.S. § 553. The EPA did provide for notice and comment regarding the draft VGP, but the petitioners argued that this was insufficient because the draft contained only the general EPA-mandated conditions for vessel discharges, but none of the more than 100 state certification conditions that were appended to the final permit. Petitioners alleged that they were therefore deprived of the opportunity to comment regarding the cumulative effects of the state conditions. The court held that petitioners failed to establish that the EPA could alter or reject state certification conditions. Thus, the additional agency procedures they demanded would not have afforded them the relief they sought. The agency correctly concluded that it could not amend or reject conditions in a state's CWA § 401 (33 U.S.C.S. § 1341) certification. Therefore, providing notice and an opportunity for comment on the state certifications would have served no purpose, and the court declined to remand to require EPA to do a futile thing.


OUTCOME: The petition for review was denied.

 

161 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Al Alwi v. Obama


No. 09-5125


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


653 F.3d 11; 397 U.S. App. D.C. 323; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 14991


November 4, 2010, Argued 
July 22, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Al Alwi v. Obama, 567 U.S. 907, 132 S. Ct. 2739, 183 L. Ed. 2d 617, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 4503 (June 11, 2012)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Appeal dismissed by In re Moath Hamza Ahmed Al Alwi, 2017 U.S. App. LEXIS 9968 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 18, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:05-cv-02223).
Moath Hamza Ahmed Al Alwi v. Bush, 593 F. Supp. 2d 24, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 105143 (D.D.C., Dec. 30, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a detainee at the United States Naval Base at Guantanamo Bay, Cuba sought a writ of habeas corpus. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia denied the petition. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Facts found by the district court were alone sufficient to conclude that petitioner was part of al Qaeda or the Taliban forces where petitioner, a Yemeni citizen, traveled to Pakistan and Afghanistan specifically to join the Taliban's fight against the Northern Alliance, stayed in at least three guesthouses closely associated with the Tlaiban and/or al Qaeda, surrendered his passport as other al Qaeda and Taliban recruits did, received training at a Taliban-related training camp, and joined a Taliban combat unit with which he fought. It was neither clear error nor abuse of discretion for the district court's determination that petitioner's own admissions, which petitioner claimed were uncorroborated and which the district court largely relied on to establish underlying facts, were reliable. So the appellate court rejected petitioner's contention that the record before the district court was insufficient to establish the lawfulness of his detention. While the district court's denial of a thirty-day continuance was hard to understand, because petitioner's attorneys had adequate time to prepare and petitioner failed to show actual prejudice, it was not error.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the district court denying the petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

 

162 of 475 DOCUMENTS


LaRoque v. Holder


No. 10-5433


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


650 F.3d 777; 397 U.S. App. D.C. 93; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 13907


May 6, 2011, Argued 
July 8, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Summary judgment denied by, Summary judgment granted by LaRoque v. Holder, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 147064 (D.D.C., Dec. 22, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00561).
LaRoque v. Holder, 755 F. Supp. 2d 156, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 134464 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff local candidate, claiming entitlement to run under a new nonpartisan system that had not received § 5 of the Voting Rights Act of 1965 preclearance, sued defendant U.S. Attorney General from enforcing § 5. Count 1 argued § 5 exceeded the Fourteenth and Fifteenth Amendment. Count 2 argued a facially unconstitutional racial-preference scheme. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed. The candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The candidate sufficiently alleged a risk of suffering concrete and particularized injuries to 2 judicially cognizable interests: that the partisan election regime made getting on the ballot more costly and time consuming and that it provided a competitive advantage to his Democratic opponents. Voters adopted nonpartisan elections, thus, he had a cognizable interest in reaping the asserted benefits of that system. His allegation of intending to run in the next election and his announcement to do so the day he filed suit showed a substantial probability of imminent injury for standing. Because § 5 prevented the city from carrying out its state-law duty to implement the nonpartisan referendum, he had both standing and a cause of action to seek declaratory and injunctive relief on the grounds that it exceeded Congress's enumerated powers. Standing on Count 2's equal-protection claim raised issues that had received little attention: if § 1973c(b)-(d) were held unconstitutional, would § 1973c(a) be left untouched, what remedy was sought, and if redressability depended on whether the city sought reconsideration of the preclearance decision. On remand, those issues were to be considered.


OUTCOME: The dismissal was reversed and remanded for consideration of the merits of Count 1. The dismissal was vacated as to Count 2 and Count 2 was remanded for further consideration.

 

163 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin.


No. 10-1227 Consolidated with 10-1228, 10-1229, 10-1230, 10-1231, 10-1232, 10-1236, 10-1237, 10-1238, 10-1240, 10-1241, 10-1242, 10-1243, 10-1244, 10-1246, 10-1247, 10-1248, 10-1249, 10-1250


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


650 F.3d 752; 397 U.S. App. D.C. 68; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 13392


May 9, 2011, Argued 
July 1, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin., 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 26425 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 16, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of Final Orders of the Transportation Security Administration.
Southwest Airlines Co. v. Transp. Sec. Admin., 554 F.3d 1065, 384 U.S. App. D.C. 325, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 1884 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner airlines sought review of final orders of the Transportation Security Administration (TSA), alleging that TSA's determination of the airlines' year 2000 screening costs was arbitrary and capricious for purposes of the Administrative Procedure Act and also unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: TSA's fees charged to the airlines for screening operations could not exceed the amount that airlines paid for screening passengers and property during the year 2000, pursuant to 49 U.S.C.S. § 44940(a)(2)(B)(i). According to the airlines, TSA's decisions were arbitrary and capricious because TSA should not have relied on a particular report commissioned by TSA, or at least should have more fully explained why it rejected the conclusions of the alternative report submitted by the airlines. The court stated that its deference to the agency was particularly strong because the statute stated that the fee was based on the amount TSA "determined" the airlines paid in 2000. Given the choice between the two reports, TSA chose the report it commissioned with good reason, and certainly sufficient reason that the court could not overturn that decision on Administrative Procedure Act arbitrary and capricious review.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petitions for review.

 

164 of 475 DOCUMENTS


In re Aiken County


No. 10-1050 Consolidated with 10-1052, 10-1069, 10-1082


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


645 F.3d 428; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 107; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 13384; 41 ELR 20222; 72 ERC (BNA) 2089


March 22, 2011, Argued 
July 1, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at In re United States DOE (High-Level Waste Repository), 2011 NRC LEXIS 8 (N.R.C., Sept. 9, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Peterson v. NRC, 411 Fed. Appx. 338, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 676 (D.C. Cir., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners filed petitions for review of and other relief from two "determinations" made by respondent Department of Energy (DOE) and other respondents: the DOE's attempt to withdraw an application it submitted to the Nuclear Regulatory Commission for a license to construct a permanent nuclear waste repository; and the DOE's apparent decision to abandon development of the nuclear waste repository.


OVERVIEW: The court found that petitioners' claim challenging the DOE's attempt to withdraw its construction license application from consideration by the Commission was not ripe for judicial determination because (1) the claim was unfit for judicial decision because, at this stage of the administrative process, the DOE had no say in whether the license application would have been reviewed and granted; and (2) petitioners' interest in prompt consideration of allegedly unlawful agency action would have been only minimally harmed by delay. Petitioners' challenge to the DOE's apparent decision to abandon development of the nuclear waste repository was not reviewable by the court because, inter alia, petitioners set forth no discrete action mandated by the Nuclear Waste Policy Act, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 10101-10270, that the DOE failed to perform or performed inadequately; the DOE's policy announcement was not a final decision or action of the Secretary or a final agency action as required by 42 U.S.C.S. § 10139(a)(1)(A) and 5 U.S.C.S. § 704; and the DOE had not failed to take any discrete agency action that Congress ordered it to take. The court lacked jurisdiction over petitioners' claims.


OUTCOME: The petitions were dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.

 

165 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Cohen v. United States


No. 08-5088 Consolidated with 08-5093, 08-5174


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


650 F.3d 717; 397 U.S. App. D.C. 33; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 13382; 2011-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50,481; 2011-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P70,303; 108 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 5046


September 29, 2010, Argued 
July 1, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Remanded by In re Long-Distance Tel. Serv. Fed. Excise Tax Refund Litig., 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 49716 (D.D.C., Apr. 10, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (Nos. 1:07-cv-00051, 06-cv-00483, 07-cv-00050).
Cohen v. United States, 599 F.3d 652, 389 U.S. App. D.C. 390, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 5207, 2010-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P50768 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporate taxpayers appealed the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia's dismissal of their Administrative Procedures Act (APA), 5 U.S.C.S. § 702, challenge to defendant IRS' illegal long-distance phone call excise taxes refund procedure. An appellate panel reversed. Rehearing en banc was granted for limited review on whether the appellate court had jurisdiction and whether the taxpayers stated a valid claim for relief.


OVERVIEW: 5 U.S.C.S. § 702 lifted the bar of sovereign immunity in actions not seeking money damages. No exception existed shielding the IRS from suit under the APA. I.R.C. § 7421(a) prohibited only those suits seeking to restrain the assessment or collection of taxes; the taxpayers' suit did not seek to restrain the assessment or collection of any tax. The assessment and collection were long-since completed and no single mechanism theory could revive them. I.R.C. 7421(a) applied only when Congress provided an alternative avenue for an aggrieved party to litigate its claims. A refund suit was not an alternative avenue for the taxpayers. "With respect to Federal taxes" in the Declaratory Judgment Act (DJA), 28 U.S.C.S. § 2201(a), meant with respect to the assessment or collection of taxes. Section § 702's waiver of sovereign immunity permitted the action and neither § 7421(a) nor the DJA otherwise limited review. Exhaustion was not required because the question of the adequacy of the administrative remedy was for all practical purposes identical with the merits of the lawsuit.


OUTCOME: Federal question jurisdiction existed and the district court was to consider the merits of the APA claim on remand.

 

166 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Ali v. Rumsfeld


No. 07-5178 Consolidated with 07-5185, 07-5186, 07-5187


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


649 F.3d 762; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 381; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 12483


January 13, 2011, Argued 
June 21, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Ali v. Rumsfeld, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 26387 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 19, 2011)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Ali v. Rumsfeld, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 26389 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 19, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 05cv01378).
In re Iraq & Afg. Detainees Litig., 479 F. Supp. 2d 85, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 21853 (D.D.C., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In actions consolidated in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, plaintiffs, Afghan and Iraqi detainees, sued defendants, the former United States Secretary of Defense and Army officers, under the Fifth and Eighth Amendments at the Alien Tort Statute, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1350, in connection with the detainees' treatment while in military custody. The district court granted defendants' motion to dismiss. The detainees appealed.


OVERVIEW: The detainees claimed that they were held at U.S. military facilities in Iraq and Afghanistan and suffered torture and abuse, including beatings, sexual assaults, and deprivation of sleep, food, and water. The court of appeals held that defendants were entitled to qualified immunity as to the detainees' constitutional claims, as it was not clearly established at the time of the alleged conduct that the detainees had any Fifth or Eighth Amendment rights. Also, allowing the detainees' claims to proceed would have presented a danger of obstructing national security policy. Because defendants were acting within the scope of their employment, the detainees' ATS claims were properly restyled as claims against the U.S. that were governed by the Federal Tort Claims Act (FTCA), 28 U.S.C.S. §§ 1346, 2671 et seq., and the detainees failed to exhaust administrative remedies as the FTCA required. The ATS claim did not fall within the exception under 28 U.S.C.S. § 2679(b)(2)(B) for statutory violations by federal employees; the ATS was strictly a jurisdictional statute and did not trigger the exception. The detainees' claim alleged a violation of the law of nations, not of a U.S. statute.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was affirmed.

 

167 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Mahoney v. Doe


No. 09-7131


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


642 F.3d 1112; 395 U.S. App. D.C. 291; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 12478


September 21, 2010, Argued 
June 21, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-00105).
Mahoney v. District of Columbia, 662 F. Supp. 2d 74, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 90231 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, including a protestor, sued appellees, the District of Columbia, a police department, and an officer, alleging that the Defacement Statute, D.C. Code § 22-3312.01, violated the First Amendment and the Religious Freedom Restoration Act (RFRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000bb et seq. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted appellees' motion to dismiss or for summary judgment. Appellants filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The protestor sought to carry out a sidewalk chalk demonstration in front of the White House regarding abortion. The department refused to grant permission to use chalk, warning that it would constitute defacement of public property in violation of the Defacement Statute. An officer confiscated the protestor's chalk and directed him to stop. The appellate court determined that the Defacement Statute was not unconstitutional as applied because (1) the District's interest in controlling the esthetic appearance of the street in front of the White House was substantial, (2) the Defacement Statute was sufficiently tailored to serve the District's esthetic interest, and (3) the District's threatened use of the Defacement Statute left him with alternative channels of communication since the District granted him approval to conduct an assembly in front of the White House. The protestor's overbreadth challenge failed because he could not show any "realistic danger" the Defacement Statute actually chilled constitutionally protected speech. The protestor's RFRA claim failed because he could still spread his message through picketing, a public prayer vigil, or other similar activities.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the district court's order.

 

168 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Omar v. McHugh


No. 09-5410


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


646 F.3d 13; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 140; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 12479


November 18, 2010, Argued 
June 21, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reissued July 8, 2011.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:05-cv-02374).
Omar v. Geren, 689 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 89006 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a military detainee set to be transferred to Iraqi custody, sought a writ of habeas corpus to avoid the transfer. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia denied the petition. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The transferee claimed he could not be transferred to the custody of Iraqi officials because he was likely to be tortured after his transfer. The U.S. Executive Branch maintained that this was not likely. The appellate court held that habeas corpus and due process did not give petitioner a right to judicial review of conditions in the receiving country. Congress was free to establish additional statutory protections with respect to transfers, whether to correspond to U.S. laws or evolving international law norms or for other policy reasons. Indeed, Congress had done so in the immigration context by allowing aliens in removal proceedings to obtain judicial review of conditions in the receiving country before being transferred. But Congress had not created such a right for extradition or military transferees. As petitioner was a military transferee and not an alien seeking review of a final order of removal under the immigration laws, the FARR Act did not afford him any right to judicial review of conditions in the receiving country.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

Nat'l Mar. Safety Ass'n v. OSHA


No. 09-1050


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


649 F.3d 743; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 362; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 12396; 23 OSHC (BNA) 1513; 2011 AMC 2849


September 14, 2010, Argued 
June 17, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Nat. Mar. Safety Ass'n v. OSHA, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 3049 (U.S., Apr. 16, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of a Final Rule of the Occupational Safety & Health Administration.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner trade association sought review of a final rule of the Occupational Safety &Health Administration (OSHA) promulgated pursuant to the Occupational Safety and Health Act (OSH Act), 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 651-678, conserning the final vertical tandem lift (VTL) Standard.


OVERVIEW: The trade association disagreed with OSHA's vertical tandem lift (VTL) Standard, arguing that (1) OSHA failed to show VTLs posed a significant worker safety risk; (2) two of the Standard's requirements were not technologically feasible; (3) the Standard was not reasonably necessary or appropriate in light of the "safe work zone" requirement; (4) OSHA's authority was limited to requiring, not prohibiting, workplace practices; and (5) the Occupational Safety and Health Act (OSH Act), 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 651-678, made an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power to OSHA. The court held that, as neither the OSH Act nor precedent unambiguously foreclosed the agency's reasonable interpretation, OSHA met its burden regarding the significant risk determination. However, the Standard was almost devoid of a feasibility analysis, containing only a bare conclusion that because all of the conditions imposed could be met by stevedores, and in fact most were being met where VTLs were currently being performed, the Standard was technologically feasible. The record lacked substantial evidence to support the feasibility of 29 C.F.R. § 1917.71(i)(9) in part and of § 1917.71(i)(10).


OUTCOME: The court granted the trade association's petition in part, vacating and remanding the Standard with respect to the inspection requirement for ship-to-shore vertical tandem lifts and the total ban on platform container vertical tandem lifts. Otherwise, it denied the petition.

 

170 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Jones v. Air Line Pilots Ass'n, Int'l


No. 10-5215


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


642 F.3d 1100; 395 U.S. App. D.C. 279; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 12393; 112 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 881


February 10, 2011, Argued 
June 17, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Jones v. Air Line Pilots Ass'n, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 26305 (D.C. Cir., July 26, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-01075).
Jones v. Air Line Pilots Ass'n, Int'l, 713 F. Supp. 2d 29, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 51483 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pilot challenged the constitutionality of the Fair Treatment for Experienced Pilots Act (FTEPA), 49 U.S.C.S. § 44729, which allowed some pilots, but not him, to take advantage of the decision to raise the mandatory retirement age. He also alleged that defendants, a former employer and a former union, violated a state age discrimination law. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the suit and the pilot appealed.


OVERVIEW: The pilot asserted it was possible for the airlines to avoid the FTEPA's nonretroactivity provision by temporarily demoting a pilot to required flight deck crew member when he turned 60, waiting for Congress to pass the FTEPA, and then promoting him to pilot. On appeal, the pilot argued that the New Jersey Law Against Discrimination (NJLAD), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-12, entitled him to such a temporary demotion. The pilot's concession to the district court that the FTEPA preempted his NJLAD claims was fatal to his appeal. No doubt recognizing that, he argued for the first time on appeal that the FTEPA did not preempt the claim he advanced on appeal: that the employer violated NJLAD by failing to temporarily demote him to required flight deck crew member so that he could take advantage of the newly enacted and higher age limit. Regardless of whether the Federal Rules' liberal pleading standard would have permitted the pilot to assert his failure-to-demote theory without amending his complaint, he failed to do so. As a result, neither defendants nor the district court had notice of the type of NJLAD claim that the pilot asserted on appeal.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the pilot's suit affirm was affirmed.

 

171 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Am. Bus Ass'n v. Rogoff


No. 10-5213 Consolidated with 10-5214


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


649 F.3d 734; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 353; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 12030


September 23, 2010, Argued 
June 14, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:10-cv-00686).
Am. Bus Ass'n v. Rogoff, 717 F. Supp. 2d 73, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 56823 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff national trade associations alleged that the Murray Amendment, Pub. Law No. 111-117, § 172 (2009), violated the First Amendment petition and Fifth Amendment equal protection rights of their members, private charter bus operators. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia agreed and ordered defendant Federal Transit Administration (FTA) to enforce the Charter Rule, 49 U.S.C.S. § 5323(d)(1). The FTA appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Murray Amendment and subsequent enactments ensured that the FTA did not spend appropriated funds to enforce the Charter Rule against any public transportation system providing bus service to Seattle Mariners baseball games through the end of fiscal year 2011. Only one entity satisfied the Murray Amendment's criteria. According to the trade associations, this compromised their members' right to petition the FTA under 49 U.S.C.S. § 5323(d)(2), and thus violated the First Amendment. There was no constitutional violation, however, because the trade associations and their members remained free to speak and petition in favor of barring the special bus service. All the Murray Amendment did was limit the FTA's authority to enforce such a bar. The trade associations also charged that the Murray Amendment violated equal protection by singling out its members for special treatment. But the Murray Amendment was rationally related to the legitimate governmental goals of accommodating handicapped fans, restoring more affordable service, and reducing traffic on game days, and thus readily passed muster under the applicable rational-basis test. Thus, there was no violation of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The district court's judgment was reversed.

 

172 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Cablevision Sys. Corp. v. FCC


No. 10-1062 Consolidated with 10-1088


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


649 F.3d 695; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 314; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 11697; 53 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 310


February 14, 2011, Argued 
June 10, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
Review of the Commission's Program Access Rules, 2010 FCC LEXIS 381 (F. C.C., Jan. 20, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a cable operator and a video programmer, argued respondent Federal Communications Commission (FCC) exceeded its 47 U.S.C.S. § 548 authority in its 2010 Order by extending its program access rules to terrestrially delivered programming, that the FCC's rules violated the First Amendment, and that certain features of the rules run afoul of § 548, the Administrative Procedure Act, and/or the First Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Because § 548(c)(2) established a floor rather than a ceiling, the FCC's reliance on § 548(b), (c)(1), to regulate conduct that § 548(c)(2) left unrestricted did not contravene congressional intent. For similar reasons, the argument that by leaving terrestrial programmers off the list of entities covered by § 548(b) Congress unambiguously placed terrestrially delivered programming beyond FCC jurisdiction was rejected. A prior regression analysis was substantial evidence supporting the presumption the § 548(c)(2)-like conduct involving regional terrestrial programming would significantly hinder the provision of satellite cable programming. There was absolutely no evidence, nor even any serious suggestion, that the FCC issued its regulations to disfavor certain messages or ideas; the First Amendment challenge failed. But, the FCC had to consider if there were relevant differences between satellite and terrestrial programming before invoking Congress's regulation of satellite withholding as a justification for treating terrestrial withholding as categorically unfair.


OUTCOME: The petitions for review were denied in part and granted in part. The court vacated that portion of the Commission's order treating certain acts of terrestrially delivered programming withholding as categorically unfair and remanded to the Commission for further proceedings.

 

173 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Marshall


No. 09-3140


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


669 F.3d 288; 399 U.S. App. D.C. 231; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 11686


May 12, 2011, Argued 
June 9, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cr-00219).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted for unlawful possession of a firearm, in violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 922(g). Despite his repeated requests for a speedy trial, his case had languished in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia for 14 months before trial finally began. The district court had relied on a government's Fed. R. Evid. 404(b) motion to suspend operation of the Speedy Trial Act's 70-day time limit, 18 U.S.C.S. § 3161(c)(1).


OVERVIEW: In the Fed. R. Evid. 404(b) filing, the government indicated that it intended to use evidence of the circumstances surrounding defendant's prior conviction for the same crime -- unlawful possession of a firearm -- as evidence that his possession of the seized weapon was knowing and intentional, not due to accident or mistake. At a status conference held on the ninety-third day following indictment (the eighty-ninth day on the Speedy Trial Act clock) defense counsel told the district court that he had not yet filed a motion to suppress the seized evidence and defendant's statement (unrelated to the prosecutor's Rule 404(b) filing) because of an unexpected delay in obtaining a needed transcript. Although the prosecutor raised Speedy Trial Act concerns, the district court stated that the Rule 404(b) filing had tolled the statute. Although defendant raised his ineffective assistance of pretrial counsel claim for the first time on appeal, the record sufficed to decide the issue and a remand was unnecessary. Defendant's pretrial counsel fell below standard in failing to object to the exclusion of the Rule 404(b) filing time in the Speedy Trial Act calculation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was vacated because defendant was denied his right under the Sixth Amendment to the effective assistance of pretrial counsel, and the case was remanded to determine whether dismissal for violation of the Speedy Trial Act should be with or without prejudice to his re-prosecution.

 

174 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Gates v. Syrian Arab Republic


No. 08-7118 Consolidated with 09-7108


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


646 F.3d 1; 396 U.S. App. D.C. 128; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 10338


November 19, 2010, Argued 
May 20, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Syrian Arab Republic v. Gates, 132 S. Ct. 422, 181 L. Ed. 2d 261, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 7352 (U.S., 2011)
Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Gates v. Syrian Arab Republic, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45327 (N.D. Ill., Mar. 29, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:06-cv-01500).
Gates v. Syrian Arab Republic, 646 F. Supp. 2d 79, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 73734 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, the families of two American contractors beheaded by terrorists, sued appellant Syrian Arab Republic under the Foreign Sovereign Immunities Act (FSIA), 28 U.S.C.S. § 1602 et seq. Syria did not respond, and the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia entered default judgment in favor of the families. Syria moved to vacate the default judgment under Fed. R. Civ. P. 60(b)(4). The district court denied the motion. Syria appealed.


OVERVIEW: Syria argued that it never received service of process because the mail carrier did not deliver it. In determining whether Syria received service of process, the district court relied on a letter the carrier sent to the families' counsel. The court found that Syria's factual argument that the carrier's letter was a fraud was speculative. The district court did not clearly err in finding that Syria received service of process under the FSIA. The families adequately effected service of process against Syria when they first filed suit under former 28 U.S.C.S. § 1605(a)(7) of the FSIA. Before the district court entered judgment, the families moved to convert their action and proceed under 28 U.S.C.S. § 1605A in accordance with § 1083 of the National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2008 (NDAA), Pub. L. No. 110-181, 122 Stat. 3 (2008). Under § 1083 of the NDAA, the families did not have to serve Syria anew because the statutory text did not treat converted claims as new claims for relief. In addition, Fed. R. Civ. P. 5(a)(2) did not apply given the FSIA's specific statutory service of process provision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

 

175 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Dearth v. Holder


No. 10-5062


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


641 F.3d 499; 395 U.S. App. D.C. 133; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 7737


November 15, 2010, Argued 
April 15, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Summary judgment granted by Dearth v. Holder, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 138697 (D.D.C., Sept. 27, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:09-cv-00587).
Hodgkins v. Holder, 677 F. Supp. 2d 202, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 504 (D.D.C., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff American citizen who resided in Canada and no longer maintained a residence in the United States and plaintiff organization sought declaratory and injunctive relief, claiming that portions of 18 U.S.C.S. § 922 and related regulations were unconstitutional because they prevented the citizen from purchasing a firearm. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the suit for lack of standing. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The citizen alleged he intended to purchase firearms in the United States for the purposes of sporting and self-defense, and to store those firearms with his relatives in Ohio. The citizen argued he suffers continuing, adverse effects sufficient to support standing because the Government denied and continued to deny him the ability to purchase a firearm. More specifically, he argued the Government denied him the ability to buy a firearm by requiring that he reside in a state as a condition of making such a purchase. The circuit court agreed with the citizen that the Government had denied him the ability to purchase a firearm and he thereby suffered an ongoing injury. The Government could not avoid suit merely by erecting a regulatory scheme that precluded the citizen from truthfully completing the application form the Government required for the purchase of a firearm. The citizen had raised a constitutional challenge under the Second Amendment to the regulatory and statutory classifications that barred him from acquiring a firearm. His desire to purchase a firearm was sufficiently actual and imminent.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing the case for lack of standing was reversed and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

 

176 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Menkes v. United States Dep't of Homeland Sec.


No. 09-5372


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


637 F.3d 319; 394 U.S. App. D.C. 385; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 4483


October 22, 2010, Argued 
March 8, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:04-cv-01456).
Menkes v. Dep't of Homeland Sec., 662 F. Supp. 2d 62, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 88736 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff pilot filed suit against defendant U.S. Coast Guard and others (collectively the Government), challenging the Coast Guard's determination to terminate his appointment as an unaffiliated, independent pilot; he claimed violation of the Administrative Procedure Act (APA), as well as his First Amendment and Fifth Amendment rights. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia granted the Government summary judgment. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: On remand from a previous appeal in the case, the Coast Guard held that, a "voluntary association" under 46 U.S.C.S. § 9304 referred to a group of people joined together for a certain purpose, and not a legal entity distinct from the persons who are members, and, therefore, the pilot had no right to serve as an independent pilot during the 2004 navigation season, because, as of December 2003, a regional pilots' association appeared to have a sufficient number of pilots to provide pilotage service for the upcoming season. The Coast Guard's interpretation of the term "voluntary association" in § 9304 easily survived review under Chevron Step Two. The pilot's First Amendment claim was barred by issue preclusion because the Second Circuit had ruled against him on the same issue in a suit against the regional pilots' association. In any event, the claim failed on the merits. The Coast Guard did not act arbitrarily and capriciously in determining that the pilot's dispatch as an independent pilot expired after the 2003 season. The pilot's Fifth Amendment due process claim was rejected, because he had no constitutionally protected entitlement to continued dispatch by the Coast Guard.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

177 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Navab-Safavi v. Glassman


No. 09-5388


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


637 F.3d 311; 394 U.S. App. D.C. 377; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 3868; 31 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1542; 39 Media L. Rep. 1417


November 5, 2010, Argued 
March 1, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-01225).
Navab-Safavi v. Broad. Bd., 650 F. Supp. 2d 40, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 79579 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractor sued defendants, the Broadcasting Board of Governors (BBG) and officials, alleging that the termination of her contract violated her First Amendment free speech and Fifth Amendment equal protection rights. The officials moved to dismiss on several grounds, including qualified immunity. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia denied the motions to dismiss. The officials filed an interlocutory appeal.


OVERVIEW: The contractor's primary duties were to translate material into Farsi and to provide "voice-over" services. When the contractor appeared in a music video that criticized the United States' involvement in Iraq, the BBG terminated her contract. Defendants contended that the BBG's interest in promoting the efficiency of its provision of public services, and more specifically, in providing the level and sort of service dictated by statute, outweighed the contractor's interest in speaking through the video and the public's interest in hearing that speech. The appellate court determined that the district court did not err in denying the officials' motions for dismissal based on qualified immunity because (1) the contractor stated a claim for violation of her First Amendment rights, (2) the appellate court was unable to determine without an evidentiary record whether any act the BBG committed in defense of its governmental functions constituted a violation of clearly established rights, or even in general terms, where the Pickering balancing tipped, and (3) her complaint was susceptible to the interpretation that her contract was terminated "because of" her ethnicity or national origin.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the district court's order and remanded for further proceedings.

 

178 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Full Value Advisors, LLC v. SEC


No. 10-1053


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


633 F.3d 1101; 394 U.S. App. D.C. 204; 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 3167


October 22, 2010, Argued 
February 4, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Full Value Advisors, LLC v. SEC, 180 L. Ed. 2d 822, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 4424 (U.S., June 13, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of Orders of the Securities & Exchange Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an institutional investment manager that wished to purchase stock and was subject to the disclosure requirements of 15 U.S.C.S. § 78m(f)(1) of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, brought suit against respondent Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC), to challenge the disclosure requirements as violative of the First Amendment and as constituting a taking in violation of the Fifth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: It was not yet certain that petitioner needed to comply with § 78m(f)(1); the SEC could have granted it confidential treatment or an exemption. If it did, the constitutional issue would not need to be resolved. Petitioner's allegedly proprietary information had not been disclosed publicly, and petitioner had not yet suffered any hardship from the requirements. When and if an exemption or confidential treatment were denied, petitioner could still seek judicial review of the SEC's decision before its filings were made public. Thus, neither the First Amendment nor Fifth Amendment challenges were ripe. As for the merits of petitioner's claim that its inability to control what the SEC did with the information it disclosed was a form of compelled speech, mere disclosure to the SEC did not raise First Amendment concerns. The requirements had a rational basis and did not require petitioner to endorse or acknowledge positions that were anathema to it. There was no public audience. Nor did disclosure raise Fifth Amendment concerns under Ruckleshaus even assuming petitioner had a cognizable property interest in knowledge of its investment positions.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the constitutional challenge to the regulations as unripe, and the court denied the challenges to the disclosure of information to the SEC as compelled speech and as a regulatory tacking.

180 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Catholic Health Initiatives v. Sebelius


No. 09-5377


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


617 F.3d 490; 393 U.S. App. D.C. 1; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 16771


May 10, 2010, Argued 
August 13, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cv-00555-PLF).
Catholic Health Initiatives v. Sebelius, 658 F. Supp. 2d 113, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 91631 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a nonprofit charitable corporation and nonprofit hospitals, brought an action under the Medicare Act to recover premiums the hospitals had paid for malpractice, workers' compensation, and other insurance. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted summary judgment to appellee Secretary of Health and Human Services. Appellants filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The Provider Reimbursement Review Board held that the investment limitations in Centers for Medicare Provider Reimbursement Manual § 2162.2.A.4 were a valid extension of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1395x(v)(1)(A) and the regulations governing reasonable cost. The district court found that the Board's adherence to the Manual's interpretation was not plainly erroneous or inconsistent with the statute or the regulation. The question was whether the Manual's investment limitations for offshore captives was an interpretive rule. Section 1395x(v)(1)(A) gave the Secretary authority to promulgate regulations defining the method or methods to be used, and the items to be included, in determining what constituted a provider's reasonable costs. The court found that there was no way an interpretation of reasonable costs could have produced the sort of detailed--and rigid--investment code set forth in § 2162.2.A.4. The connection between § 2162.2.A.4 and reasonable costs was simply too attenuated to represent an interpretation of those terms as used in § 1395x(v)(1)(A) and the regulations. Section 2162.2.A.4 could not have been sustained as a valid interpretation of reasonable cost.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed. The case was remanded to the district court with instructions to set aside the decision of the Board and for such other relief as the district court deemed appropriate.

United States v. Maynard
