Motion denied by United States v. Jones, 800 F. Supp. 2d 90, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 85052 (D.D.C., 2011)
Affirmed by United States v. Jones, 132 S. Ct. 945, 181 L. Ed. 2d 911, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 1063 (U.S., 2012)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Maynard v. Wilson, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 169535 (E.D. Va., Nov. 26, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:05-cr-00386-ESH-10).
United States v. Jones, 451 F. Supp. 2d 71, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 56473 (D.D.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION:  The court reversed one defendant's convictions and affirmed the convictions of the other defendant.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, defendants challenged their convictions in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia after a joint trial for conspiracy to distribute and to possess with intent to distribute five kilograms or more of cocaine and 50 grams or more of cocaine base, in violation of 21 U.S.C.S. §§ 841 and 846. One defendant also challenged the sentence imposed by the district court.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected defendants' joint arguments that the district court erred in (1) admitting evidence gleaned from phone wiretaps, (2) admitting evidence arising from a search incident to a traffic stop, (3) denying their motion to dismiss the indictment as invalid, (4) declining to instruct the jury on their theory that the evidence at trial suggested multiple conspiracies, and (5) declining to grant immunity to several defense witnesses who invoked the Fifth Amendment to the Constitution of the United States and refused to testify. Reversing as to one defendant, the court agreed that the district court erred in admitting evidence acquired by the warrantless use of a GPS device to track that defendant's movements continuously for a month. The court held that United States v. Knotts did not govern the case and the police action was a search because it defeated defendant's reasonable expectation of privacy. Without the GPS data, the evidence that defendant was actually involved in the conspiracy was so far from "overwhelming" that the court held that the Government had not carried its burden of showing the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court reversed one defendant's convictions and affirmed the convictions of the other defendant.

Koretoff v. Vilsack


No. 09-5286


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


614 F.3d 532; 392 U.S. App. D.C. 215; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 16036


April 22, 2010, Argued 
August 3, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Koretoff v. Vilsack, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 25409 (D.C. Cir., Dec. 13, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-01558-ESH).
Koretoff v. Vilsack, 601 F. Supp. 2d 238, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 19494 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff almond producers and retailers sued defendant U.S. Department of Agriculture, arguing that 7 C.F.R. § 981.442(b), promulgated under the Agricultural Marketing Agreement Act of 1937(AMAA), 7 U.S.C.S. §§ 601-674, was arbitrary and capricious, exceeded agency statutory authority, and violated procedural requirements. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia dismissed the suit. The producers and retailers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The rule required use of approved methods for reducing salmonella bacteria in almonds, all involving either pasteurization or chemical treatment. 7 C.F.R. § 981.442(b). The current dispute arose because the rule had the effect of largely eliminating the domestic raw almond market. Inter alia, the appellate court held that the AMAA did not preclude producer suits challenging rules and orders issued under it. Indeed, precluding producers' suits would effectively undermine the presumption in favor of judicial review that the U.S. Supreme Court had consistently reaffirmed. The government's argument that handlers could sue and that was good enough for producers was inconsistent with bedrock tenets of administrative law. However, the retailers' argument that the regulation improperly required them to exhaust administrative remedies was a challenge to the agency's exhaustion requirement that had to be raised first to the agency. As the retailers' administrative remedies were not exhausted, the district court properly dismissed their claims.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the judgment of the district court with respect to the suit of the ten producers, so their claims could go forward. The appellate court affirmed the district court's judgment dismissing the claims of the three producer-retailers, which first had to be raised first to the Department of Agriculture.

United States v. Baugham


No. 07-3145


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


613 F.3d 291; 392 U.S. App. D.C. 126; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 15775


November 16, 2009, Argued 
July 30, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by United States v. Baugham, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27504 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 24, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Baugham v. United States, 131 S. Ct. 1510, 179 L. Ed. 2d 334, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 1695 (U.S., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 01cr00253-02).
United States v. Baugham, 449 F.3d 167, 371 U.S. App. D.C. 213, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 13761 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of various federal drug and conspiracy offenses. He appealed his three concurrent 20-year sentences and $ 1,000 fine, arguing that the United States District Court for the District of Columbia failed to observe the requirements of 21 U.S.C.S. § 851(a) and (b) when it enhanced his sentence based on a prior drug conviction and that it levied a vindictive fine on him in violation of his constitutional due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Because defendant failed to preserve his 21 U.S.C.S. § 851(a) and vindictive sentence claims before the district court, those claims were subject to plain error review. The inclusion of a co-defendant's name in the body of the § 851(a) pre-trial information instead of his own did not render the information invalid under § 851(a). It was undisputed that the prosecutor filed and served in a timely manner an information detailing defendant's prior convictions. Thus, the prosecutor strictly complied with § 851(a)'s requirements. Defendant failed to show prejudice. Under a de novo standard of review for defendant's § 851(b) claim, the district court erred by not conducting the required colloquy, and the government conceded as much. However, the government carried its burden of showing that the error did not substantially harm defendant by affecting his sentence. At no point had defendant indicated that the drug conviction on which his sentence enhancement depended was invalid in any way. Defendant unsuccessfully argued that the imposition of the fine at resentencing created a presumption of vindictiveness since the district court had not imposed a fine in his original sentence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was affirmed.

United States v. Burroughs


No. 08-3085


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


613 F.3d 233; 392 U.S. App. D.C. 68; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 14652


February 26, 2010, Argued 
July 16, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cr-00126-RJL-1).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia sexual exploitation of a minor, 18 U.S.C.S. § 2251(a); transportation of a minor to engage in prostitution, 18 U.S.C.S. § 2423(a); and first degree child sexual abuse, D.C. Code § 22-3008. He was sentenced to a term of imprisonment followed by supervised release. On appeal he challenged his sentence, including several special conditions of his release.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that his lawyers should have had him evaluated by a mental health expert before he was sentenced, but his claim of ineffective assistance failed because he had not raised a colorable claim of prejudice under the second prong of Strickland. Because defendant did not oppose the challenged supervised release conditions at any point--not in his sentencing memorandum, not in allocution before the sentence was pronounced, and not after it was imposed--the circuit court reviewed for plain error under Fed. R. Crim. P. 52(b). Defendant did not use a computer to facilitate his crimes. The government did not recommend that the court impose any supervised release conditions related to computers, but the Probation Office did. Neither the Probation Office nor the district court explained its reasons for restricting defendant's computer access. Not knowing the court's reasons for imposing these conditions, finding the government's reasons unsupported by the record, and unable to identify any itself, an appellate court vacated the conditions as plainly out of sync with the relevant factors and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The supervised release conditions that required defendant to allow the monitoring of his computer use and to maintain a daily log of all addresses accessed by way of any computer, other than those authorized for employment, were vacated and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the opinion. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed.

People's Mojahedin Org. of Iran v. United States Dep't of State


No. 09-1059


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


613 F.3d 220; 392 U.S. App. D.C. 55; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 14647


January 12, 2010, Argued 
July 16, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at In re People's Mojahedin Org. of Iran, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 7173 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 10, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of an Order of the Department of State.
Nat'l Council of Resistance of Iran v. Dep't of State, 373 F.3d 152, 362 U.S. App. D.C. 143, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 14187 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent United States Secretary of State issued an order designating petitioner, a Muslim organization and its aliases, as a Foreign Terrorist Organization (FTO). When the Secretary denied petitioner's request for revocation of the FTO designation, petitioner sought review.


OVERVIEW: After reviewing an administrative record with both classified and unclassified information, the Secretary denied the petition. Ordinarily, the court would have had to decide whether to set aside the denial based on the adequacy of the record. However, a review was not sufficient to supply otherwise absent due process protection of notice to the organization and an opportunity for a meaningful hearing, which did not happen here. Petitioner was notified of the decision and permitted access to the unclassified portion of the record only after the decision was final. Even though petitioner was given the opportunity to include in the record its own evidence supporting delisting, it had no opportunity to rebut the unclassified portion of the record the Secretary was compiling. Despite the Secretary's claims, there was no basis for relaxing due process requirements and the failure to provide required notice and unclassified material in advance of the decision was not harmless. The court left the designation in place but remanded with instructions to provide petitioner the opportunity to review and rebut the unclassified portions of the record on which the Secretary relied.


OUTCOME: The court remanded with instructions to allow petitioner the opportunity to rebut the unclassified portions of the record.

Fawzi Khalid Abdullah Fahad Al Odah v. United States


No. 09-5331


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


611 F.3d 8; 391 U.S. App. D.C. 378; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 13822


April 6, 2010, Argued 
June 30, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at, Motion denied by, As moot Fawzi Khalid Abdullah Fahad Al Odah v. United States (In re Fawzi Khalid Abdullah Fahad Al Odah), 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 13838 (D.C. Cir., July 7, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Odah v. United States, 131 S. Ct. 1812, 179 L. Ed. 2d 772, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 2740 (U.S., Apr. 4, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:02-cv-00828-CKK).
Fawzi Khalid Abdullah Fahad Al Odah v. United States, 648 F. Supp. 2d 1, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 78222 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, detained at Guantanamo Bay pursuant to the Authorization for Use of Military Force, Pub. L. No. 107-40, 115 Stat. 224 (2001), through his next friend filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus in the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. The United States District Court for the District of Columbia denied the petition. Defendant and his next friend appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his next friend contended that the preponderance of the evidence standard employed by the district court was unconstitutional, that it was error to admit hearsay evidence, and that the evidence was insufficient to show that the detainee was part of al Qaeda and Taliban forces. The appellate court held that the law of the circuit was that a preponderance of the evidence standard was constitutional in considering a habeas petition from an alien detained pursuant to AUMF authority. Decisions of the instant court and the Supreme Court established that in this type of habeas proceeding, hearsay evidence was admissible if reliable, which the disputed evidence was. The record strongly supported the district court's finding that defendant was part of al Qaeda and Taliban forces. He traveled to Afghanistan on a series of one-way plane tickets purchased with cash. Once there, he located and followed a Taliban official, spent time at a Taliban training camp, did what the official recommended to him, including giving up his passport and other possessions, obtaining an AK-47 rifle, and participating in a march with armed men that was attacked by United States and allied warplanes.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the district court's denial of defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

188 of 475 DOCUMENTS



GE v. Jackson


No. 09-5092


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


610 F.3d 110; 391 U.S. App. D.C. 299; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 13223; 40 ELR 20170; 71 ERC (BNA) 1033


May 18, 2010, Argued 
June 29, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, Rehearing, en banc, denied by GE v. Jackson, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27485 (D.C. Cir., Sept. 30, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by GE v. Jackson, 131 S. Ct. 2959, 180 L. Ed. 2d 245, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 4334 (U.S., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:00-cv-02855-JDB).
GE v. Jackson, 595 F. Supp. 2d 8, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 5379 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Columbia granted summary judgment to appellees, an agency administrator and agency, after appellant company challenged on due process grounds a statutory scheme that allowed the agency to issue orders, known as unilateral administrative orders, directing companies and others to clean up hazardous waste for which they were responsible. The company appealed.


OVERVIEW: The company had received multiple unilateral administrative orders (UAO), issued under the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation, and Liability Act (CERCLA) statutory scheme by the agency, which directed companies to clean up their hazardous wastes. Under that scheme, a potentially responsible party (PRP) could comply and, after the cleanup, seek reimbursement from the agency, as contemplated by 42 U.S.C.S. § 9606(b)(2)(A), or refuse to comply and allow the agency under 42 U.S.C.S. § 9606(b)(1) to file a federal trial court enforcement action, or have the agency cleanup the site and seek to recover costs, as contemplated by 42 U.S.C.S. § 9607(c)(3). It sued to challenge CERCLA's UAO regime and alleged a violation of the Fifth Amendment because the regime allowed the agency to issue orders without a hearing or, alternatively, because issuance of the order could have harmful financial consequences. The appellate court rejected both due process challenges, the first because a company could refuse and force the agency to seek a remedy in the federal trial court and the second because the possible financial consequences were not sufficient to merit due process protection.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to the agency administrator and agency.

Gonzalez v. DOL


No. 09-5195


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


609 F.3d 451; 391 U.S. App. D.C. 242; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 12739


March 18, 2010, Argued

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Gonzalez v. Dep't of Labor, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27514 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 26, 2010)
Rehearing, en banc, denied by Gonzalez v. DOL, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27515 (D.C. Cir., Aug. 26, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cv-00593).
Gonzalez v. DOL, 603 F. Supp. 2d 137, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 25921 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a federal employee who suffered a work-related injury and her husband, sued defendant, the U.S. Department of Labor, after the Office of Workers' Compensation Programs (OWCP) issued a decision that plaintiffs were to reimburse to defendant its subrogation interest from a settlement received from third-parties. The U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia granted summary judgment to defendant. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Office of Workers' Compensation Programs (OWCP) found that the settlement proceeds were jointly paid to both plaintiffs, that the husband was entitled to retain 25% for his loss of consortium, and that defendant was due a payment in an amount less that what it had paid to plaintiff in benefits. Plaintiff wife, as a beneficiary of benefits under the Federal Employees' Compensation Act (FECA), 5 U.S.C.S. § 8101 et seq., was required to reimburse defendant for those payments. Clearly, the settlement was for the joint benefit of both plaintiffs, not just the husband for his loss of consortium. Defendant's 25% allocation for loss of consortium was neither unauthorized nor arbitrary; it was by the book. Defendant was not estopped from seeking reimbursement because it did not actively participate in the litigation against the third parties. A requirement that defendant participate in litigation in order to preserve its reimbursement rights would have been inconsistent with one of the purposes of FECA's reimbursement scheme. Finally, defendant's denial of certain costs to plaintiff's lawyer as not litigation-related was not arbitrary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's grant of summary judgment to defendant.

El-Shifa Pharm. Indus. Co. v. United States


No. 07-5174


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


607 F.3d 836; 391 U.S. App. D.C. 51; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 11585


December 16, 2009, Argued 
June 8, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by El-Shifa Pharm. Indus. v. United States, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 829 (U.S., Jan. 18, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 01cv00731).
El-Shifa Pharm. Indus. Co. v. United States, 402 F. Supp. 2d 267, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 35436 (D.D.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a pharmaceutical plant owner and its principal, appealed the judgment of the United States District Court for the District of Columbia, which dismissed its claims against defendant, the United States of America. Plaintiffs brought suit seeking a declaration that the United States violated international law and asserted claims for defamation.


OVERVIEW: In 1998, plaintiffs' pharmaceutical plant was destroyed following a missile strikes conducted by the United States after two foreign embassies were bombed. President William Clinton had ordered the missile strikes. Plaintiffs' request for compensation from the Central Intelligence Agency was denied and they filed the underlying action. Plaintiffs sought a declaration that international law was violated by the United States failing to compensate them for the unjustified destruction of their property. Plaintiffs also claimed that they were defamed by President Clinton and senior officials, who linked them to international terrorism. The court upheld the district court's dismissal of the action based on the political question doctrine. The court held that the case at hand involved the decision to launch a military strike abroad, which raised nonjusticiable political questions. The court noted that the defamation claim similarly required the court to determine the factual validity of the Government's stated reasons for the strike. The court determined that it could not assess the merits of President Clinton's decision to launch an attack on a foreign target.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's dismissal of plaintiffs' claims.

Kiyemba v. Obama


No. 08-5424 Consolidated with 08-5425, 08-5426, 08-5427, 08-5428, 08-5429


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


605 F.3d 1046; 390 U.S. App. D.C. 429; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 10967


April 22, 2010, Argued 
May 28, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Kiyemba v. Obama, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 2826 (U.S., Apr. 18, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Remand from the U.S. Supreme Court.
Kiyemba v. Obama, 555 F.3d 1022, 384 U.S. App. D.C. 375, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 3013 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, Chinese citizens who were being held as detainees at Guantanamo Bay Naval Base, Cuba, sought writs of habeas corpus seeking to compel respondent government to release them into the United States. On remand from the U.S. Supreme Court, the court considered the government's motion to reinstate its prior judgment.


OVERVIEW: The U.S. Supreme Court remanded for a determination of what proceedings were necessary for the full and prompt disposition of the case in light of new developments. The new developments the Court identified included the fact that all 17 detainees had received at least one offer of resettlement in another country, and 12 had accepted an offer. The remaining five rejected three offers and were still being held at Guantanamo Bay. On remand, and after oral argument, the appellate court held that even if the remaining five detainees had good reason to reject the offers, they had no legal right to be released into the United States. In addition, an intervening opinion of the appellate court precluded the sort of judicial inquiry the detainees sought. It was for the political branch, not the courts, to determine whether a foreign country was appropriate for resettlement. Intervening congressional legislation prohibiting the expenditure of funds to bring any Guantanamo detainee to the United States did not violate the Suspension Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 2, nor was such legislation an unlawful bill of attainder.


OUTCOME: The court reinstated its judgment and opinion, as modified, to take into account new developments.

 

192 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Maqaleh v. Gates


No. 09-5265 Consolidated with 09-5266, 09-5267


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


605 F.3d 84; 390 U.S. App. D.C. 352; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 10384


January 7, 2010, Argued 
May 21, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Maqaleh v. Gates, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27670 (D.C. Cir., July 23, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeals from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:06-cv-01669-JDB).
Maqaleh v. Gates, 604 F. Supp. 2d 205, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 49437 (D.D.C., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, three detainees at a U.S. military base in Afghanistan, appealed a judgment from the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia denying them habeas corpus relief from confinement as unlawful enemy combatants. The district court had certified the suit for interlocutory appeal under 28 U.S.C.S. § 1292(b). Appellant government filed a petition for an interlocutory appeal pursuant to that certification.


OVERVIEW: The detainees were citizens of Yemen and Tunisia who were captured in Afghanistan, Pakistan, and Thailand and held as military prisoners at a U.S. Air Force Base in Afghanistan. The district court held that § 7(a) of the of the Military Commissions Act of 2006, Pub. L. No. 109-366, 120 Stat. 2600 (2006), purported to deprive the court of jurisdiction, but that it did not constitutionally apply to deprive the court of jurisdiction under the U.S. Supreme Court's test in Boumediene v. Bush. Considering the jurisdictional question on interlocutory appeal, the court applied the three-factor Boumediene v. Bush test and held that the district court lacked jurisdiction to consider the petitions for habeas corpus. The jurisdiction of the courts to afford the right to habeas relief and the protection of the Suspension Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 2, did not extend to aliens held in Executive detention in a military detention facility in the Afghan theater of war. The government did not exercise de jure sovereignty over the air base--unlike on its base on Guantanamo Bay, Cuba--but held the detainees pursuant to a cooperative arrangement with Afghanistan on sovereign Afghan territory.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the district court and ordered that the detainees' petitions for habeas corpus be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.

BNSF Ry. Co. v. Surface Transp. Bd.


No. 09-1092 Consolidated with Nos. 09-1190, 09-1234


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


604 F.3d 602; 390 U.S. App. D.C. 294; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 9609


February 18, 2010, Argued 
May 11, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by BNSF Ry. Co. v. Surface Transp. Bd., 131 S. Ct. 2441, 179 L. Ed. 2d 1208, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 3630 (U.S., 2011)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by BNSF Ry. Co. v. Surface Transp. Bd., 2014 U.S. App. LEXIS 1882 (D.C. Cir., Jan. 31, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of Orders of the Surface Transportation Board.
Western Fuels Ass'n v. Surface Transp. Bd., 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 12062 (D.C. Cir., June 3, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor shippers challenged rates charged by petitioner railroad. Respondent Surface Transportation Board found that the rates were unreasonably high. The Board prescribed future maximum rates and ordered the railroad to pay reparations. The railroad petitioned for review of the Board's decision.


OVERVIEW: The shippers claimed that the railroad's rates for coal shipments were unreasonable under the Board's stand-alone-cost test, which required design of a hypothetical stand-alone railroad (SARR). The proceedings were held in abeyance while the Board developed new methodologies for evaluating rates, which applied retroactively. The shippers then redesigned their SARR, and the Board found that the railroad's rates were unreasonable. The railroad claimed that the proceeding should have been dismissed because the three-year time limit under 49 U.S.C.S. § 11701(c) expired prior to the Board's decision. The court of appeals held that the railroad forfeited the three-year limit argument by failing to timely raise it before the Board; the railroad did not raise the issue until after the three-year period had purportedly run. The Board's decision to allow the shippers to redesign their SARR by rerouting traffic was not arbitrary and capricious, as the decision was consistent with Board precedent. However, the Board did not specifically address the railroad's objection that the Board's modified average total cost methodology caused variable costs to be counted twice.


OUTCOME: The railroad's petitions were granted as to the double-counting objection, and the matter was remanded. The petitions were otherwise denied.

Newdow v. Roberts


No. 09-5126


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


603 F.3d 1002; 390 U.S. App. D.C. 273; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 9370


December 15, 2009, Argued 
May 7, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing, en banc, denied by Newdow v. Roberts, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27590 (D.C. Cir., June 29, 2010)
Rehearing denied by Newdow v. Roberts, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 27591 (D.C. Cir., June 29, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Newdow v. Roberts, 131 S. Ct. 2441, 179 L. Ed. 2d 1235, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 3573 (U.S., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:08-cv-02248-RBW).
Newdow v. Bush, 391 F. Supp. 2d 95, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 19909 (D.D.C., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff atheists appealed the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia's judgment dismissing a declaratory and injunctive relief suit against defendants, the Chief Justice of the United States, other public officials, clergy, and presidential inaugural committees, which alleged that inclusion of religious expression in the 2009 and future inaugural ceremonies violated the First Amendment's Establishment Clause and the Fifth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The atheists objected to the President-elect's inviting ministers to lead prayers at his 2009 inaugural ceremony and his request that the Chief Justice conclude the oath of office with the phrase "So help me God." The atheists also sought to enjoin inclusion of similar religious elements in future presidential inaugural ceremonies. The district court had denied a preliminary injunction. Two similar suits filed by the lead plaintiff had been dismissed due to his lack of standing. The instant suit was dismissed after the 2009 ceremony was held. On appeal, the court held that inclusion of other plaintiffs in addition to the lead plaintiff sufficed to confer standing. The suit was mooted with regard to the completed 2009 inaugural. Even assuming that the situation was capable of repetition, the atheists were barred from asserting that the issue evaded review because they failed to appeal the denial of their preliminary injunction motion. The atheists lacked standing with regard to future inaugurals because there was no certainty that religious expression would be included in those ceremonies, as the content of inaugurals fell exclusively within the discretion of each President-elect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

195 of 475 DOCUMENTS


Am. Trucking Ass'ns v. EPA


No. 09-1090


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


600 F.3d 624; 390 U.S. App. D.C. 93; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 6848; 40 ELR 20110; 70 ERC (BNA) 1289


October 2, 2009, Argued 
April 2, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petition for Review of a Final Action of the Environmental Protection Agency.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner trucking industry association sought review of a decision by respondent, the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA), approving California's proposed rules governing certain in-use non-road engine emissions pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 7543 of the Clean Air Act.


OVERVIEW: The California standards applied to transportation refrigeration units (TRUs) powered by diesel engines. Cal. Code Regs. tit. 13, § 2477(b) required all TRUs on vehicles operating in California to comply with the emissions standards. EPA approved the rule pursuant to § 7543(e)(2)(A)(i)-(iii), which meant that all other states could either adopt an identical rule or were prohibited from regulating emissions from such engines. The association argued that EPA erred in applying § 7543(e)(2)(A)(ii), which required a finding that California needed the rule at issue to meet compelling and extraordinary conditions in California. The court of appeals held that EPA reasonably interpreted the statute and found that California continued to suffer from some of the worst air quality in the nation. Nor did EPA err in applying § 7543(e)(2)(A)(iii), which required California's proposed rule to not be inconsistent with the rest of the statute. The rule did not apply anywhere but in California and did not apply to vehicles that did not operate in California. EPA also adequately considered the costs of compliance with the California rule as required under 42 U.S.C.S. § 7521(a)(2).


OUTCOME: The association's petition for review was denied.

 

196 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Mahdi


No. 03-3154


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


598 F.3d 883; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 374; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 6493


December 7, 2009, Argued 
March 30, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Mahdi v. United States, 131 S. Ct. 484, 178 L. Ed. 2d 306, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 7964 (U.S., 2010)
Post-conviction relief denied at, in part United States v. Mahdi, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 166892 (D.D.C., Nov. 25, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 01cr00396-01).
United States v. Mahdi, 2007 U.S. App. LEXIS 9002 (D.C. Cir., Apr. 16, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Convicted on multiple drug, Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO), 18 U.S.C.S. § 1962(d), murder, and firearms charges in connection with his drug enterprise in the District of Columbia (D.C.), and sentenced under the Violent Crimes in Aid of Racketeering statute (VICAR), 18 U.S.C.S. § 1959, to 10 life sentences with two consecutive sentences, defendant appealed from the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia.


OVERVIEW: VICAR penalties were separate from and in addition to what was authorized by any state or federal law, based upon an additional statutory element -- the underlying violent offense being related to racketeering. VICAR did not authorize cumulative punishments. The D.C. murder conspiracies were charged merely as racketeering acts within the RICO count; there was no multiplicity. The 18 U.S.C.S. § 924(c) gun counts were not multiplicitous with D.C. Code § 22-4504(b) gun counts because each required proof of a fact the other did not. The Commerce Clause was irrelevant to applying VICAR in D.C. as U. S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 17, art. IV, § 3, cl. 2, gave Congress plenary authority in D.C. No remand was needed for treating the U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual as mandatory because § 1959(a)(1), not the Guidelines, mandated life for the VICAR conviction. Even if there was a merger, the D.C. convictions would merge into the federal counts; the federal mandatory sentences would not change. But, limited resentencing remand was proper because 6 distribution of, or possession with intent to distribute convictions merged into the analogous drug convictions for being near a school.


OUTCOME: The convictions and sentences were affirmed, with the exception that the conviction on two counts of distribution of a controlled substance and four counts of possessing with intent to distribute (PWID) a controlled substance, which together merged into six corresponding counts of distribution and PWID within 1,000 feet of a school, were vacated, and the case was remanded for resentencing.

Cablevision Sys. Corp. v. FCC


No. 07-1425, Consolidated with 07-1487


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


597 F.3d 1306; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 317; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 5296; 2010-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P76,977; 38 Media L. Rep. 1449; 49 Comm. Reg. (P & F) 1002


September 22, 2009, Argued 
March 12, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On Petitions for Review of an Order of the Federal Communications Commission.
In re Implementation of the Cable Television Consumer Protection and Competition Act of 1992, 22 F.C.C.R. 17791, 2007 FCC LEXIS 7082 (F.C.C., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioner cable television companies sought review of a decision by respondent Federal Communications Commission to extend for five years the prohibition under 47 U.S.C.S. § 548(c)(2)(D) of the Cable Television Consumer Protection and Competition Act of 1992 against exclusive contracts between cable operators and cable affiliated programming networks.


OVERVIEW: Section 548(c)(2)(D) directed the Commission to promulgate regulations prohibiting exclusive contracts for cable and broadcast programming between a cable operator and a cable programming vendor in which the cable operator had an interest. The exclusivity provision was subject to a sunset provision under § 548(c)(5) under which it would lapse after 10 years unless the Commission found that the prohibition continued to be necessary to preserve and protect competition and diversity in the distribution of video programming. The Commission extended the exclusivity provision in 2002 and again in 2007. The companies challenged the 2007 extension. The court held that § 548(c)(2)(D) had been found facially constitutional under the First Amendment, and the companies failed to raise an as-applied challenge. The Commission reasonably interpreted the meaning of "necessary" under § 548(c)(5) in finding that the prohibition should continue. The Commission found that the ability and incentive for vertically integrated cable operators to withhold programming remained substantial enough to require further extension of the exclusivity prohibition, and that finding was entitled to deference.


OUTCOME: The petitions for review were denied.

Carr v. District of Columbia


No. 08-7083


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


599 F.3d 653; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 391; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 24556



March 11, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Carr v. District of Columbia, 587 F.3d 401, 388 U.S. App. D.C. 332, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 25482 (2009)

 

199 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Vinton


No. 07-3125


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


594 F.3d 14; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 199; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 2450


December 10, 2009, Argued 
February 5, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Vinton v. United States, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5909 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 06cr00298-01).
United States v. Vinton, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 9938 (D.D.C., Feb. 10, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, convicted of narcotics and firearm offenses after the contraband was found in a briefcase in his car during a traffic stop, appealed the United States District Court for the District of Columbia's denial of his motion to suppress. He argued the evidence was discovered during an unconstitutional search of his vehicle and property in violation of the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was properly stopped because a police officer's firsthand observations gave him probable cause to believe that defendant had been speeding and driving with windows tinted in excess of the legal limit. Upon approaching defendant's car, the officer saw a knife with a five-and-a-half-inch sheath in plain view on the backseat, easily within reaching-distance of defendant. Although the officer removed the knife and placed it out of arm's reach on the roof of defendant's car, he was justifiably concerned that additional weapons might be hidden elsewhere in the vicinity. While knowledge of a recent double-stabbing homicide in the same neighborhood was not itself sufficient to justify a protective search, it added to the circumstances warranting a search of the car. Finding a second knife concealed under a floor mat provided probable cause for a warrantless arrest of defendant for violation of D.C. Code § 22-4504(a). The officer was reasonable in expecting there might be additional weapons in the car, particularly in a locked briefcase found on the backseat. Also, it was reasonable to believe evidence relevant to the crime of arrest might be found in the locked briefcase.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

200 of 475 DOCUMENTS


United States v. Carter


No. 08-3009


UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA CIRCUIT


591 F.3d 656; 389 U.S. App. D.C. 71; 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 971


September 22, 2009, Argued 
January 15, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Carter v. United States, 130 S. Ct. 3308, 176 L. Ed. 2d 1208, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 4136 (U.S., May 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the United States District Court for the District of Columbia. (No. 1:07-cr-00215-RBW-1).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a 150-month sentence imposed by the United States District Court for the District of Columbia upon entry of his guilty plea to five counts of bank robbery in violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 2113(a).


OVERVIEW: The length of defendant's sentence was based, in part, on the finding that his criminal history under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 4A1.1 included four Maryland theft convictions. Defendant argued that the certified state court computer records proffered by the Government were insufficient to prove prior convictions by a preponderance of the evidence. The court rejected defendant's contention that the records were unreliable. The records constituted competent evidence under U.S. Sentencing Guidelines Manual § 6A1.3, and the mere possibility of error did not warrant the conclusion that the records were inherently unreliable. Further, the fact that the records did not cite the statute under which defendant was convicted did not render them inadequate for the criminal history calculation. Finally, the court rejected defendant's contention that Shepard and Apprendi established a requirement that the Government prove prior convictions only through a conclusive judicial record of conviction. The court joined its sister circuits in concluding that Shepard did not apply when determining whether the Government had satisfied its burden of proof as to the existence of prior convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence.
